THE RED PAGE .
Nicholson’s “Halek.”
. . . philosophical reflections, ivhich the critics de-
clared to be so obvious fromwhat had preceded them that,
although they were given in the most elegant language,
only the dullest of the Pagamas would find it necessary
to read them.— Halek.
Imprimis : you must not call it “Halick
as if it were a little vulgar boy; but “ Hab-lek.
“Halek ” is the life-book of John H. Nicholso
an amiable, aimable old gentleman living
Queensland, where he formerly taught school,
small first edition was printed in London soi
dozen years ago : the second is just issued by 1
J. Ross and Co., of Brisbane. Of late yea
“Halek” has been unattainable by the ordinar;
reader, and while the author’s enthusiastic d
ciples lauded it as the masterpiece of Australi
literature the ordinary reader could only concea
his ignorance and pine. He need not pine an;
longer: he need never have pined at all.
“Halek,” upon its title-page, is called a
mance : it is really a bad allegory—which is
abomination. Bad, for this reason : that an al
gory is merely a figure of speech, an extendec
metaphor, whose whole object is to simplify anc
enforce the presented thought—generally by gi vir
abstract ideas (which are for most minds difficu
to comprehend) a concrete form (which is
Figurative expression really complicates a simp
statement; but you complicate in order to sii
plify, reculez pour mieux sauter, tightly coil
loose mental spring in order that the force o:
recoil may be the greatest possible. A metaph
is a turf thrown (like this) to make the though
geyser spout.
But too many turfs do not stimulate, they bu
the geyser ; and it is one of the most difficu
things in the world to invent a long allegor
which shall not bury the thought. Bunyan ma
aged it : and how? Partly by personifying on!
the moods and qualities familiar to the averag
man, and giving them all the plain, well-unde
stood names which belonged to them—“ Faith
ful,’’ “Worldly-Wise,” “Giant Pope,” “Gian
Despair.” These names are perpetual guide
posts: even if the picture does not look like
horse, you have Bunyan’s plain “This is a horse
scrawled underneath to set your mind at ease
But Mr. Nicholson has invented a half-Hebrew
jargon which one has to puzzle out before begin
ning to comprehend his allegory. He deals wit
Karomas and Pagamas, with Khoshek and Kas
hep and Boded, with Abdan and Zenah and
Benuben and Bak. Your mind has perpetually
to stop and ask itself “What is Anek ? ” o
“Why should they do this in Sahitam? ” lb i
like continual dictionary-reference when reading
foreign language : all the vitality goes out of th
matter.
Mr. Nicholson, in fact, has deliberately thrown
up a barrier between his intelligence "and th
reader’s, and he sits behind it while his disciple
call him profound. He isn’t; he is merely
obscure. “ Halek ”is Hebrew for “ pilgrim,” anc
the book is an inferior “Pilgrim’s Progress.” I
purports to describe the spiritual history of an
individual, apparently the author. The main
phases are allegorised as countries through which
the pilgrim journeys : the people be meets are the
influences which help or retard his spiritual pro
gress. The story is by no means without merit
though its merit is of a rather commonplace
stolid kind ; but it has one crowning fault—it is
insufferably tedious. Chief causes : Complexity
introduced by the jargon before-mentioned ; com
plexity of the author’s ideas, less suited to allegory
than Bunyan's more simple ones ; fact that the
book is conceived and written on the plane of
mediocrity; alienation from the author’s far-back
intellectual standpoint.
* * * *
The “ Halek ” read for the purpose of
this notice was a first-edition copy once owned by
Francis Adams. Adams seems to have rather
approved of “Halek,” and he wrote this sonnel
on the fly-leaf :
This is the book of God’s sweet inner life
Where heart and soul are one with loveliness.
The toiler weary-hearted, in the press
of nature jangled and man’s famished strife,
findeth the hour of quiet and relief:
the gentle worker stricken with distress
heareth again the speech of those that bless.
This is the book of God’s sweet inner life.
Ah weary-hearted toiler, know thy peace,
that peace that tells of quiet soltice yet
and Karom’s hope of heavenly increase I
O worker gentle, all thy doubt forget
in that clear joy whose music ne’er shall cease
with eyes and feet glad Sahitamwards set.
And Adams marked the following passage :
Methought I stood on the sea-beach ; the air was
strangely clear with an amber-colored light; purple
shadows lay on the hills; no sound was heard ; and
everything appeared to be hushed in expectation. And
now the Beautiful Maiden stood before me ; but my soul
was held entranced, so that I could not speak to her.
She looked with a solemn tenderness into my eyes, and
gave me a white flower of great beauty; but while I
looked thereon, as though wondering what it should sig-
nify, the golden light faded, the sea and the hills
vanished, and I awakened.
It would require many words to show whv this dream
so greatly comforted me; and it may be thought that the
sorrows of a love whioh had fed on a glance might easily
be solaced by a dream ; but from this dream I took the
assurance that I should again behold and have fuller
intercourse with the Beautiful Maiden ; and that in the
meantime I should guard and cherish the purity of that
love which she had planted in my breast.
Upon this passage and the chapter in which it
occurs Adams commented : “Not since the Vita
JVuova has love shone with so pure and sweet a
glance as this.” But he didn’t read the book. He
jibbed at page 129, after getting through rather
less than one-third.
* * * *
Macaulay defends Bunyan against the
charge of tedium and denounces Spenser, “Ver;
few and very weary,” he says, “are those whoar
in at the death of the Blatant Beast” in the
“ Faerie Queen.” Craik passes the complimen
to Milton, and enquires for the survivors at the
end of Book XII. of “ Paradise Lost.” Further
he quotes somebody else as dubbing “ Pilgrim’s
Progress” “mean, jejune, and wearisome” —in
defiance of Macaulay. One wishes Craik hac
named that somebody else, for he was evidently
critic to be loved. “The Pilgrim’s Progress” is
on the level of a childish intelligence (though
even as a child the writer found the “Holy
War” much more interesting): its value to
the mature mind is purely as a literary
performance. One can admire the graphic way
in which Bunyan makes his pictures : the vivic
felicity of his style. But modern thought has
made his data, and his argument, and his con-
clusion almost worthless. There is no doubt that,
owing to the religious character of their work,
both Bunyan and Milton (as epic poet) have beer
ridiculously over-valued. Now we are beginning
to get them in their proper literary perspective.
It is curious to see how, lowjo intervallo, it is
the same with Nicholson. His mind is hobbled
in a creed; his book is the utmost hop of his
hobbled mind ; and he has apparently enlisted all
the black-coats of Brisbane to puff his book.
These puffs, by some astounding blunder of taste,
are printed as a preface—“in order,” says the
author, “ that the general public, at any rate of
Queensland to begin with, may see what is
thought of ‘ Halek ’ by some of those whose
opinions are worth having.” In other words,
“All these notable people admire ‘ Halek.’ Don’t
expose your ignorance by disagreeing with them.”
The virtuous “ Halek ” himself would find this
intolerable. And there is also prefixed a puffing
“ review ” which says :
Those who study “ Halek ” will find it a work invalu-
able as a companion, soothing in its effects upon irritated
nerves, strengthening' to the weak, a gentle curb upon
the proud, and at all times a noble incentive to the
leading of a life which shall reflect credit upon the in-
dividual, etc.
It reads like an advertisement of Somebody’s
Safe Cure.
* * * »
The good thing about “Halek” is the
style : the thought is poor. The style is sin-
gularly simple, lucid, sonorous, dignified, re*
strained. It wants enthusiasm, variety, and
dynamic force (by reason of the want of dynamic
thought). It has music, but the music is always
in the same key :it grows monotonous. Biblical
English has evidently been Mr. Nicholson’s model,
touched up with Ossian ; and his own English has a
certain Biblical majesty : there is a large air about
it :it is patriarchal and impressive. It is a fine
vehicle—if he had a better load. But Mr.
Nicholson has little pith or originality : he invents
nothing : he never gets away from commonplace
facts and conventions—all his art is to variegate
them and disguise them with uncouth names.
Even the marvellous maiden Turoni, living in the
exalted state of Karom, is pulled by precisely the
same strings as the ordinary Mary Jane Smith of
social commerce. When Halek comes to her
(they are poor, but love one another devotedly)
and suggests joining pittances and marrying, she
prefers to wait so that “ we could build a larger
house,” and so that Halek might earn enough
money to enable her to give up basket-making.
So Halek gets a railway-pass from the Labor
Bureau and goes mining up-country in order
to make his fortune, with the result that
while he is away Turoni dies and his life
becomes a misery till he gets religion. If he
were an intelligent pilgrim, he would under-
stand that religion is a mighty poor substitute for
the nice warm wife Turoni would have made
him : as he is merely Mr. Nicholson’s lay-figure,
he takes what he gets and professes to be thank-
ful.
All the figures in “Halek” work according to
Mr. Nicholson’s notion of the eternal unfitness of
things. It is a poor notion—the old one that man
is built all wrong, that this earthly existence of
which alone he knows anything is devised princi-
pally to wash the iniquity out of him and
hang him out to dry, that his proper home
is somewhere in the skies, and if he has any savee
he’ll light out for that home as quick as possible,
and that meanwhile the more nice things he denii s
himself the-better. A.kind of human morning-
mist theory, now—thank goodness ! —rapidly dis-
persing. Man’s built just right; all his progress
has come through stretching out to satisfy his
appetites, which want only regulating, not morti-
fying ; and the time to be happy is now, the place
to be happy is here.
Oh threats of Hell and Hopes of Paradise 1
One thing at least is certain— this Life flies ;
One thing is certain and the rest is Lies ;
The Flower that once has blown for ever dies.
• * * »
Thus a g#od half of Mr. Nicholson’s alle-
gory is wasted : it deals with the Things that
Don’t Matter. Same with Banyan ; same with
Milton. So much space spent in mere fetish
words and formulas set forth on the weary ole
basis of creedal assumption. There is no help, no
value in these Don’t-Matter-Things : doubtless
they comfort illiterate sectaries who can swallow
the initial dogma; they delude impulsive people
with weak heads ; but for anyone else they are
mere shells of shibboleths hiding each its vacuum
The thoughts of Milton, for example, don’t stanc
on cardinal human facts, emotional or intellectual,
as Shakespeare’s thoughts do. His men anc
women are not real live men and women like
Homer’s, but didactic divines arguing from im-
probable premises to an irrational conclusion.
Bunyan is better, because his tinker’s-religion
was less a matter of learning, more of feeling (how
he would have loved “ the Army !”) Nicholson
is better still, because lie is later, and religions
move on at the bidding of Policeman Science like
everything else. The half (say) of his work which
is in effect an exhortation to live on a higher
spiritual plane is good enough to read when one
has plenty of leisure to chew it. It has a little
suggestive stimulus—generally, it is true, at
second-hand. Thus the prefatory puffer quotes
“ the following musical description of sound and
color ” as unsurpassed in English prose
Behold a tree standing alone upon a bare mountain.
The sun rises and the tree is transfigured, while his
leaves, waving in the breeze, glitter like jewels.
Sometimes the wind brings him from afar the perfume
of trees of his own kind, which he has never seen. Then
do his bright leaves quiver and droop.
But this is merely Heine’s hackneyed
Ein Fichtenbaum steht einsam
spoilt in paraphrasing. And the puffer quotes
this as “ one of the grandest passages in the whole
work ” :
Then did the music rise and fall, and rise again, pulsing
in solemn rhythm, till I saw the island no more, but alone
on some serene height I took into my heart the fierce
pangs, the pining sorrows, the loneliness, and the grand
endurance of those bright spirits who hope and have not,
who toil without sympathy, who fight without victory,
but who still strive upwards, urged and sustained by the
glorious instinct that at last, somewhere, it shall be well
with them.
Which combines a familiar idea from Lucretius :
Suave, mari magno, &c.
and an idea from Browning
One who never turned his back, but marched breast for-
ward.
Never doubted clouds would break,
Never dreamed, though right was worsted, wrong would
triumph,
Held we fall to rise, are baffled to fight better,
Sleep to wake.
And so on, and so on.
* * * *
All the same, a copy of “ Hal ek ” should
be sent to Gladstone by every mail until he bites.
The book only wants his fillip to boom for a gene-
ration as “ a Christian classic,” and its sale-possi-
bilities with the people who add up the numbers
in Proph. Daniel are simply immense.
Items.
Ernest Robin’s “ The Clairaudient ”
(Angus and Robertson) is an agreeable booklet,
making “copy of what lies around you.” The
first few chapters are rather interesting ; but the
Mahatma is a bore, and the interest wanes to the
close.
H. H. Champion claims to have published
the first English translation of Ibsen’in a little
u I* 1 called To-Day (now defunct),
which he edited in London in the earlv eighties
JLhe play was “ A Doll’s House,” done by Frances
Lord, and published as “ Nora.”
Randolph Bedford, reasonably hard-up,
some time ago sent a batch of short stories to
U° nd - P ublisher Unwin. Accepted; but would
Mr. Bedford kindly tone down several of the
stones, herewith returned as not in accordance
with Propriety? -Jeshurun, however, had by
this mail fattened into a Westralian mine-pro-
moter and capitalist, and he kicked. “The
Dpinions of Dunwin’s Deader,” it was putconclu-"
sively, “were possibly highly Dsteemed by his
own Dittlo Dircle, but they carried no Height
in the Dosmopolis of Dhite Deather, W.D.A.”
As of author to publisher, the attitude has novelty
—and some charm.
One advantage of publishing in Australia
seems to be that English critics look with more
curiosity to an Antipodean book, and give it
better notices than those doled to the big batch
from the publisher-on-the-spot. Louise Mack’s
English notices to hand are very perfunctory and
paragraphic, though distinctly flattering. And
“The World is Round ” may now be had again
at the local booksellers’: the first supply ran con-
siderably short of demand.
W. A. Squire, author of a little pamphlet
with a big name—“ Ritual, Myth, and Customs
of the Australian Aborigines; a Short Study in
Comparative Ethnology ” (Robert Blair, Mait-
land, N.S.W.) —has swallowed a lot of interesting
miscellaneous facts which he now disgorges any-
how. He seems compiler, not observer; yet his
references to authorities are so erratic that one
never feels safe with him. And he has some
undigested interest.

‘corduroy ’ key-note in the first line of his book
of verses—
Tramp, tramp, along the verandah and turn.
Also that, in order to make one decent poet, eye-
rhymer Heney and ear-rhymer Holdsworth should
be (in the new word) amalgated.”

BEFORE YOU
T. E A. P!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: ‘‘There be land-sharks and water-
sharks, ” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer-won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

A Friendly Game of Football.
FOUGHT AT BUNGAREE.
We were challenged by the Dingoes —they’re the
pride of Squatter’s Gap—
To a friendly game of football on the flat by
Devil’s Trap,
And vve went along on horses, sworn to triumph
in the game
For the honour of Gyp’s Diggings and the glory
of the same.
And we took the challenge with us. It was
beautiful to see,
With its lovely curly letters and its pretty
filagree ;
It was neat and gently worded, and it made a man
feel good,
For it breathed the purest sentiment of peace and
brotherhood.
We had “Chang” and Timmy Hogan, and the
man who beat the Plug ;
Also Heggarty, and Hoolahan, and Peter Scott,
the pug.;
And we wore our knuckle-dusters, and we took a
keg on tap
For our friendly game of football with the fellows
at the Gap.
All the Dingoes came to meet us, and we spoke
like brothers dear.
They’d a tip-dray full of tucker, and a waggon-
load of beer;
Likewise lint and sticking-plaster —and we all
expected fun
Ere our friendly game of football with the Dingo
boys was done.
The umpire was an ugly man, a stranger to the
push,
With large, distorted features and a flavor of the
bush.
He said he didn’t know the game, but spat and
swore an oath
To see ail square between the teams of knock the
hide off both.
Then we bounced the ball and started, and for
twenty minutes quite
We played the game with courtesy and‘proper
sense of right,
When Fitzpatrick fired Macdougal on his brisket
in the mud,
And that hero rose up chewing dirt and famishing
for blood.
But Simonsen, the umpire, sorted out the happy
pair,
And he found a pitch to suit them, and he left
them fighting there ;
And then Halligan and Cop-out met with cries of
wrath and pain,
And wild horses couldn’t part those ancient
enemies again.
The umpire dragged them panting to a quiet little
patch,
And then gave his best attention to the bruising
and the match, .
And I never wish to come across a fairer-minded
chap
For a friendly game of football than that umpire
at The Gap.
In due time young Snlith and Henty, and Blue
Ben, and Dick, and Blake,
Chose partners from the Dingoes, and went slug-
ging for the cake.
Then Tim Hogan hit the umpire, and was promptly
put to bed _ /
’Neath the ammunition waggon, with a bandage
on his head.
Feeling lonely-like, Magee took on a local star
named Bent,
And four others started fighting to avoid an argu-
ment.
So Simonsen postponed the play for fear some
slight mishap
Might disturb the pleasant feeling then prevailing
at The Gap.
And some fifteen minutes later twenty couples
held the floor,
And the air was thick with whiskey, and the
ground was tinged with gore,
And the umpire kept good order in the interests
of peace,
Whilst the people, to oblige him, sat severely on
the p’lice.
We fought the friendly game out, but no man
could say who won.
We were candidates for shutters when the glorious
day was done ;
Both the constables had vanished—ope was carried
home to bunk —
And the ump'ro was exhausted, and the populace
was drank.
But we’ve written out a document, with Father
Finney’s aid,
Breathing friendship and affection, and the cpal-
lenge is conveyed
To the Dingo Club at Squatter’s, and another
friendly game ' . .
Will come off here at the Diggings—we re in train-
ing for the sanie.
We have pressed The Gap to bring their central
um]lire if they can,
As we all admire him warmly for a fair and decent
And we reckon on a lively game beside the I pcenix
For “The Giant Killer’s ” guaranteed to plug him
if he comes.
EDWARD DYSON.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to Tiie Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C. ; Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus ; Street A Co., Cornhill.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outride N.S. Wales is now £l, with 3s. 3d.
added for postage. England £l, with 6s. 6d. added for
postage.

prepaid. There ivill be no exception to this rule.
SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 5, 1896.
A Way Out.
The present Maorilancl Government, which
tackles more big questions with a light heart
than any other Ministry in Australasia, pro-
poses to try its hand at the construction of a
workable Parliamentary system. Hitherto
Maoriland, like the other six provinces, has
possessed a system which was wholly un-
workable in theory, and which only struggled
through after a cumbrous sort of fashion in
practice because one or other of the parties
to the frequent deadlock was generally talked
into, or scared into, surrender or compromise,
or gave in from corrupt or other motives, or
died, or grew tired, or the question at issue
dropped and people forgot that there was a
deadlock. One of the curiosities of the
beautiful political policy which Australia in-
herited from Britain is that it makes all
manner of provision for creating deadlocks
between the two Houses of Parliament, and
no provision at all for remedying them. Its
great aim and object is to bring an irresist-
able force into violent concussion with an
immovable post on a plain, and leave them
there. In most cases of violent disagree-
ment some way out is ultimately discovered,
after days or months or years of talk and
worry and agitation, but there is no recog-
nised way out. The Constitution is a ten-
storey house built without stairs, the reason
assigned for its construction being that, as a
person can’t well spend his whole existence
on a top storey, he will probably find a way
to get down. The idea of supplying a way
down is contrary to the Constitution.
* * * *
Whether the Upper House is nominee or
elective, the situation is much the same. The
Assembly represents the popular will so far
as it can be ascertained at the polls. It is
understood in a more or less hazy fashion
that the popular will must prevail. When
the Upper House merely endorses the opinion
of the popular Chamber, then it is a useless
excrescence —a voice saying ditto in the
wilderness —a mere echo which the country
could do as well without. When it reverses
the vote of the popular Chamber it contra-
venes the first great maxim of the country’s
political system. The people don’t govern,
and the democracy is a failure. The country
elects the Assembly ; the Assembly creates
the Ministry ; the Ministry, more or less
directly, creates the Council in those pro-
vinces where that institution is run on the
nominee system ; and the Council has power
to reverse the decisions of the nation, the
Assembly, and the Government all combined.
The people keep the nominee Council pre-
sumably that it may do something, and when-
ever it is found doing anything except saying
ditto to the popular House—which is not
doing anything—there is a great and labori-
ous effort to suppress it. The Assembly re-
presents the public opinion of to-day. The
nominee Council represents the successive
layers of public opinion for the past twenty
or thirty years. Ministries and Parliaments
have come and gone during that period, but
the permanent legislators which they have
created remain, and the nominee Council is
merely the debris left on the shore of politics
by successive high tides of public opinion.
When this institution, which was presumably
created for the purpose of doing something,
is found doing anything, the country proceeds
to suppress it in a miserably circuitous
fashion by calling it bad names in the papers,
holding public meetings, delivering speeches,
dissolving the Lower House—which has
nothing to do with the matter —appealing to
the Governor to create a new batch of
nominees to vote down the ones already in
hand, which new batch will create similar
trouble later on, appealing to the British
Government to make the Governor appoint
the said nominees, and other riot and excite-
ment. There is no authorised solution of
the difficulty, but after everybody has talked
everybody else nearly to death something
generally happens to smooth matters over.
If it doesn’t the subject drops from sheer
exhaustion.
* * * *
Where the Council is an elective institu-
tion with a property franchise, as in Victoria
and Tasmania, the situation is even more
difficult. There the people in general are
represented in the Assembly. Most of them
are also represented in the Council, but
owing to the fact that only landlords are
eligible to sit in the House of Fossildom, and
that in the absence of any salary only
wealthy landlords can afford to go there,
they are driven in most cases to elect
M.’s.L.C. whose sentiments are hopelessly
antagonistic to popular opinion. When these
persons insist, allegedly on behalf of then-
constituents, upon undoing what the same
constituents have instructed their other re-
presentatives in the Assembly to do, the re-
sult is the most complete deadlock on rcord.
The M.’sL.C. can’t ioe swamped by new
nominees. There is nothing to be gained by
bringing pressure on the Ministry, or the
Governor, or the Governor’s bosses in Eng-
land, for there is no way in which any of
them can interfere There is nothing to be
gained by dissolving the Assembly and tak-
ing the sense of the people, for the Council
only accepts instructions from its own con-
stituents at its own elections. The Council
can’t be dissolved, no matter how outrageous
its conduct may be. If there is in the
Assembly a 60 to 1 majority in favor of some
urgent reform, and in the Council a majority
of one against it at a sitting where only a
dozen are present, it is lost, even though the
two Houses put together show a vote of three
to one in its favor. The whole system is
contrived to create two Houses which are
calculated to take diametrically opposite
views of almost everything, and then to leave
them there.
* * * *
The Maoriland Government proposes to
meet the difficulty by a simple expedient
which has already been tried in various
countries and found reasonably efficient.
When any measure has twice within fourteen
months been passed by one House and re-
jected by the other, the House which passed
it may, within three weeks thereafter, call
on the Governor to summon a convention of
the two Houses sitting as one, and leave the
question of passing, amending, or rejecting
the measure to the majority. Some of the
details of this scheme are clumsy and ridi-
culous, but the general idea is ex-
cellent. It creates a court of appeal
from the eternal deadlock. So long as
the popular chamber is the more numerous
of the two it provides a means whereby the
people, if they are in dead earnest, can over-
rule the Propertied House, and also, if they
are sufficiently in earnest, a means whereby
they can amend or abolish it. It is not an
absolutely logical plan ; it practically pro-
vides that a two-thirds majority (or there-
abouts) of the Assembly may overrule the
Council’s veto, and this being so the obvious
course would be to enact that it should do so
without any joint sitting—only the necessity
for not treading too heavily all at once on
the moral bunion of the Council probably
makes it desirable to adopt the more circuit-
ous and more respectful course. Also, seeing
that the Maoriland Council, if it refuses to
vote as it is told, may be bullied into
obedience by swamping it with new members,
and that it is now further proposed to place
it under the domination of a big majority in
the Assembly, it is openly acknowledged
that the Council is not expected to have any
will of its own, and that the country can’t
afford to let it have one. In other words,
long experience has proved that the Council
is no good as a house of revision, for it cheer-
fully passes all the swindles and land-jobs
and disastrous loans, and all the foolish
proposals which don’t affect its private in-
terests, and only rejects those which affect
its private interests, and the only power
with which it can safely be trusted is
the power to say ditto to the popular
Chamber. Therefore, the Government's pre-
sent project aims at making it say ditto mote
regularly than heretofore. This being the
acknowledged state of affairs,, the reason for
keeping an expensive institution merely
that it may be laboriously clubbed into a
constant state of unanimity is not apparent;
but the bi-cameral system is a British fetish
of great magnitude, and it is probably good
policy to deal with it by instalments. The
Council may be deprived of all real authority,
but its name and some limited powers of
obstruction will still remain, and the theory
that a Legislature must consist of two
branches, each violently contradicting the
other, will be satisfied.
* * * *
The weak point about the scheme is its
too great elaborateness. The provision that
a Bill must be twice passed and twice re-
jected within fourteen months to bring it
within the operations of the Act, will only
incite the Council to fill in time on the
second appearance of the measure with use-
less discussion, postponements, amendments,
inquiries before select committees, and. so
on, till it gets the date of actual rejection
past the 14 months’ limit. The proposal
that the Bill must be twice rejected by the
same House will incite the Council, the
second time a liberal measure comes before
it, to pass it with impossible amendments,
and thus compel the Assembly to do the
rejection. The fact that the demand for a
combined sitting of the two Chambers must
come within three weeks of the second rejec-
tion looks as if it could be easily dodged,
by simply allowing the measure to drift into
oblivion a second time a few minutes before
the prorogation, in which case there would
not be within the next three weeks any House
to make the required demand. Or the
Opposition in the Assembly might fill in the
specified three weeks with a want of confi-
dence motion, and thus prevent any demand
being made. There is no visible reason why
there should be two rejections before the
matter can come before the combined
Houses, or why they should necessarily
happen within 14 months, or why the other
House should have to demand its court of
final appeal within three weeks instead of
being allowed to make it any time during
that or next session. Still, with all these
complications, the measure is the first
serious attempt to provide a way out of the
political deadlock, and is commendable on
that account. Hitherto the only method of
treating the deadlock has consisted in the
two parties howling for each other’s blood
at a distance, and any solution—whether
by tossing up, or by a hundred yards’ race
between the Speaker of one House and the
President of the other, or by a combined
vote, or by hard drinking (the House which
provides the last man capable of sitting up
to carry its point), or by the referendum, or
by any other means which will produce a
decision—has at least some semblance of de-
finiteness to recommend it. Even a process
which is as likely to decide the wrong way as
the right one is better than one which gives
no decision at all either way.
Britain and Free-Trade.
On 25th June last it was exactly 50 years
since the British House of Lords passed the
Bill to repeal the Corn Laws. Fifty years of
the “blessings of Freetrade” ! Fifty years of
progress towards the era of national brother-
hood and universal prosperity ! Fifty years
during which British population, wealth, and
trade have enormously increased ! Obviously,
if there is any virtue in theories, any value in
long rows of figures, Britain is the most enviable
country on earth. Yet Britain is uneasy.
* * * *
The repeal of the Corn Laws sacrificed the
British agriculturist to the British manu-
facturer. During the last half-century, chiefly
by reason of Freetrade, the cultivated area of
the United Kingdom has been gradually chang-
ing its character Tilled land has given place
to pasture land, and pasture land to woods and
plantations. The-change has been most marked
in recent years Between 1880 and 1894, the
area under corn crops decreased by 1,500,906
acres ; under green crops by 283,538 acres ; of
other crops by 106,939 acres; of uncropped
arable land by 291,570 acres During the same
period the area under grass increased by
2,510,406 acres; the area of woods and
plantations by 206,003 acres. These figures
are compiled from the Financial Reform
Almanac for 1895—a Freetrade authority.
Even Freetraders do not deny the inferences to
be drawn from them. That British land is.
going out of cultivation is a fact so evident to
observers that the corroboration of statistics is
hardly needed.
* * * *
Yet Britain’s continental rivals have main-
tained or increased their arable areas. This
recent comparison is significant :
France. Germany. Belgium. U.K.
Percentage of arable
land to total area.. 49-20 48'46 53-48 29T7
Possibly, with juster land-laws, the British
agriculturist might be able to continue the
struggle. Certainly, were the landlord abol-
ished, a comparatively moderate measure of
protection would be needed. When British
agriculture paid, the landlord skimmed the
cream of the profits; now that there is no cream
he takes the milk, and the farmer and laborer
get only a hand-to-mouth living, if that. No
practicable protection will help them till the
burden of rent is shifted or reduced. Even
before the Corn Laws were .repealed, the posi-
tion of the actual agricultural worker was only
a little better than at present. Then—he
existed; now—he almost exists. But the
difference, though slight, is material.
* * * *
The position now is that, with British agri-
culture dead or dying, British manufactures
also are beginning to sicken. Though popula-
tion has increased, imports and exports have
both fallen heavily since 1890, and the enor-
mous disparity between them is widening.
Between 1890 and 1894 exports of British
produce declined from £1 os. 7d. to £5 11s. 3d.
per head of population. Allowance for
financial crises and the fall in values will
not account for this enormous decrease. And
again figures are endorsed by facts. The great
iron trades and cotton trades complain of acute
depression. Other branches of industry are
similarly despondent. Germany and Belgium
are beating Britain out of African and
Australian markets. India and Japan are
driving her from the markets of “ the
East.” Since 1880 British exports of cotton
manufactures, of glassware, of arms and ammu-
nition, of linen manufactures, of iron and steel,
of salt, of silk manufactures, have decreased
both in quantity and value. English politicians
are beginning to realise that they have in vain
sold the yeoman’s birthright for a mess of com-
mercial pottage, if the pottage is to be little by
little taken away by foreign competitors.
* * * *
Meanwhile, the hated label “made in Ger-
many ” is becoming more and more common in
Britain; the stream of German immigrants,
ousting British clerks and shopmen from their
places, flows incessantly; and Freetrade Britain
sees her markets swamped with foreign pro-'
ducts, while foreign protective duties prevent
her from retaliating. Consider this table :
Percentage of Imports Received from the U.K. by

This table is compiled from the Financial

therefore inferences from it are unaffected by
trade crises or price fluctuations. The com-

for which the F.R.A supplies figures) is fair,
because, though the gross amount of one
country’s trade with all other countries may
fluctuate from year to year, for temporary
reasons, the percentage of its gross trade which
it does with any individual country is unlikely
to be temporarily affected to a serious extent
by any cause but war—a cause which cannot
here be invoked. So that, not temporary,
but persisting causes are in operation to com-
paratively reduce the volume of imports taken
from Britain by the countries mentioned. And
the table shows that the most important foreign
nations and the three most important of British
possessions have materially decreased their
imports from the United Kingdom during the
last six or eight years. (The decrease of 1 per
cent, in German imports, for example, means
a loss of about £2,000,000 in annual trade-
value to the U.K.) Exceptions are Norway,
Sweden, Greece, Italy, Spain, Roumania, the
Argentine, Chili, Mexico, the Cape, Ceylon,
Mauritius, West Indies, British Guiana, and
Natal; but the average is seriously against
Britain.

Put in words, this means that Britain’s chief
continental rivals are exporting to her propor-
tionally more goods while importing from her
proportionally fewer ; and that Britain, on the
whole, is importing and exporting proportion-
ally less from and to her colonies and dependen-
cies—that is, she is doing less trade with her
own kinsfolk. Obviously, the foreign manu-
facturer and artisan are profiting at the expense
of the British manufacturer and artisan ; and
as the total figures previously given likewise
show—Britain is losing trade all round.
* * * *
To sum up, Britain’s manufacturing industries
have commenced to decline, and unless she can
find fresh outlets for her products, or in some
way hinder the progress of her rivals in the old
fields, or reserve her home markets for herself,
the decay of her manufactures will follow upon
the decay of her agriculture, and the glory of
John Bull will have departed. These are some
of the results of the great Freetrade doctrine.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Vaccination and Vacillation.
The “Jenner Centenary”—the 100th anni-
versary of the first vaccination —occurred in
May this year, and was enthusiastically honored
by the leading medical associations of U.S.A.,
Berlin, and St. Petersburg. The Centenary
was also celebrated in a semi-official manner by
the old English town of Gloucester, which had
long been a head-centre of the anti-vaccinists.
The method of celebration was a smallpox epi-
demic which attacked about 2000 persons. A
violent reaction set in at once in favor of vac-
cination, and in a few days 25,000 out of a total
population of 42,000 (mostly “conscientious
objectors ”) submitted to the “ filthy nostrum
of the quack Jenner” —included in the ’verts
was a local editor who had long cursed the rite,
and who published his recantation. It is now
cabled that the Royal Commission which has
been sitting for seven years has reported that
“ vaccination is a necessary protection against
smallpox” and also records its opinion that
the law should not operate again t a child

The subject has two aspects —scientific and
political. The scientific question is of a
sort that is often left to experts —but the poli-
tical question is one that an Australian com-
munity would prefer to settle for itself. As
things are in Australia, small-pox is almost a
flegatidd. Occasionally an ocean-steamer brings
a ease td oUr shares—bilt the quarantine regula-
tions cornet it, and it ceases by effluxion of
time. But Sydney had its scare soide years
ago, when <£30,000 Was sp'erit iri buildings and
£54,000 on the care o'f patients and on! the isola-
tion of those suspected of, the contagion'. Small-
pox has to b,e imported—unlike typhoid arid
such fevers, which develop op the spot. Prac-
tically Australia has escaped small-pox iri fchb
past—but, if it ever does get a footing here, as
in England, then will be recognised the neces-
sity to turn to vaccination as a help in time of
trouble. The Royal Commission has found
against “compulsory” vaccination. The first
question involved is whether the State or the
parent has paramount interest in the welfare
of the child. The State already insists
that children’s births shall be registered, that
the children shall be sent to school, etc. If
the parents raised “ conscientious objections”
ip these things it would be without avail. The
State riot drily protects the child from cruelty,
b'rit it iiisisis (flat it shrill get the opportunities
of education. This is as much iri the State’s*
interests as the child’s. Tlie State decides that
ignorance is a danger. Similarly, the State cap
decide that non-vaccination is a danger—and
as far as the issue between State and child is
concerned there is no matter of conscience—it
is only the third party, the parent, that has
scruples. If vaccination is a good thing, and a
necessary protection, it is only weakness that
refrains from carrying it to a logical conclusion.
The Fiscal Referendum.
Labor-leader M‘Gowen agitated N.S. W.
Assembly, the other day, with a proposal for
submitting the weary fiscal issue to a referen-
dum, and thereby settling it definitely for five
years. The question which he suggests should
be put to the electors is simply : “ Will you
have Freetrade or Protection for five years
certain?” Whichever policy the country
decides for both parties are to unite loyally ill
carrying it out to the best of their ability, and
thereafter tile qriestion is to drop Until next
tieritiir'y. There ate some good points abortt
this propdsitioli, and sorrle that ate not so' godd
!jy a lorig wdy; The edriritry warits a referen-
dum which Will serve all purpose's ; riot a special
oiie which cari b'e Used only once, find on tile
fiscal qiiestiofi only. The drily tisib'le reason!
why a special referendum shorild be got up foT
the fiscal question in particular is that it will get
M‘Gowen and others out of a bad hole and lift
a ldt df responsibility off them. The Labor-
leadei’, like nriariy df his partisans, is a Protec-
tidnist arid alsd a supported df the land and
iiicoirie tax, and therefore tlie workings of the
party systeni left hint in ;l very Uwkward
dileiiima. The Lyne erdwd offered an instal-
ment of Protection and no direct taxation ; the
ReiD erdwd offered ail instalment of direct
taxation and no Protection ; and after mature
deliberation as to which fragment of a loaf the
party should accept it decided, rto doubt cort-
ecieUtidUsly, that the ReiD fragment was the
larger df the two. Bub still there is an
feeling that a big responsibility was incurred
in choosing between the two fragments, and the
choice may have been ill-advised, and a special
referendum will give the people a chance to grab
at the Lyne crust as well if it wants to, and
thereby ease the party of its moral burden.
The Bulletin, for its part, doesn’t see that
there is much to be gained by giving the people
a direct vdte only once on one question only.
Moreover* the people can’t by one vote relieve
M’Gowen and eo. front all responsibility on the
fiscal issue for five years 5 the voters who will
be dead three years hence can’t bind the new
voters who will have come of age by that time
to believe in any fiscal creed for two years
longer, and to deprive the new voter who
comes on the roll, say, in 1898, of his right to
re-settle the fiscal issue until 1901, is simply
disfranchising him for three years so far as that
question is concerned. And he will probably
want to know what power any dead men had
to disfranchise him on any one question
Without his consent. And, in any case,
the object of the referendum is to give the
people a direct vote on all important subjects ;
nob simply to relieve M'Gowen and Co. of the
one difficulty which won’t let them sleep at
night. Farther, if a single vote is taken on
this one issue one party must be extinguished,
and the probable result will be that that party
will be against the referendum thereafter, and
the complete application of the principle will
be further postponed. The Bulletin doesn’t
believe, as a general thing, in the cry of “ All
or nothing,” but it regards the present case as
one in which that axiom applies, and it hereby
treats the proffered half-loaf with contempt.
Trie Law and the Profits.
Dear Bulletin, —The Law Almanac is not
exciting reading ; but anyone who turns up the
list of barristers practising in N.S. Wales will
be staggered by the number of men who must
be dragging out a miserable existence in this
high-toned profession. How many of them
make a living God knows ! That is only one
aspect of the matter, though, and does not in
the least concern the public, who would nob
care if the whole of the profession were on
rations, or in Liverpool Benevolent Asylum.
Another is the want of interest which these
gentlemen show in all matters political, social,
and intellectual. In other countries, notably
England and America, the members of the Bar
have always taken a prominent part in every
movement, political or social, which had for its
object either the guidance or the education of
the people. Why do we find such a different
state of affairs here ? You can count on your
fingers the barristers of N.S. Wales who are
known to the public either by their work in
Parliament or on the platform. The reason is
not far to seek. Barristers now have lost sight
of those high ideals which are alleged to have
once animated the profession. Their vision is
Dow limited to the figures marked on their
briefs. The men who grab every bit of work
that comes in their way ; keep every nerve on
the strain lest, in a moment of relaxation, si
guinea should be dropped ; who burn the mid-
night oil in the attempt to grasp the dollars ;
these are not the persons to whom the public
can look for leading or for guidance. This
being the case with the leaders, the effect
of it on the junior members is to cause
them to follow ou the same track. They, in-
deed, do not scoop in the cash ; but they live
irt hopes of doing so, and in hopes of nothing
rilse'. Political knowledge, intellectual attain-
ments,' ciiltiire as a it hole, have no charms for
them ; tlffiir o'nly aim is td “ make money,” and
the' result is the seditions attention paid to
solicitors vHio' niay, if carefully cultivated, send
tjhetn work the acceptance of speculative briefs
for which 1 , the he ate Ho’ fees unless the case be
won ; and a! general sap'p'iffg of the independ-
ence of the profession. There are' exceptions
here, as there are to every rule ; but the excep-
tion is always said to prove the rule. To sum’
up : the members of the Bar have almost de-
generated into keepers of law - shops, and
second-hand shops at that, without an idea or
an aim beyond their business and the profits
thereof. Until they raise themselves above this
old-clo’ level, they will claim in vain the dis-
tinction of belonging to a learned profession,
and will continue to receive that scant respect
from the public at large which is their proper
due.— Shark.

Tlier Halfpenny Aspect @f Things.
Sydney Da ifit’ Tel-egraph has been collecting
the opinions of a vast number of Sydney and
suburban aldermen arid mayors,- arid other
heavy functionaries, as to the practicability o'f
the proposed County Council. Their answers
are, in the main, very much alike. The follow-
ing, from a YVoollahra potentate, is a fait
sample:
There are some very good features in connection with
it, but 1 think the formation of a full County Council is
premature. A County Council works very well where
there is an amalgamation of old and settled communities.
But here, you see, the great stumbling block is the ques-
tion of finance, The taxation of the city of Sydney, as
levied by the corporation, is Is. 4d. in the £. The taxation
in the suburbs is about the same. But the city of Sydney
is able, out of this Is, 4d., to provide for all its require-
ments. A portion of the suburban boroughs are in a state
of bankruptcy, and, if the city amalgamates with these
boroughs, and they cannot get on with this Is. 4d., there
are only two courses open, Either they will have to in-
crease the taxation in the suburbs, or the city must assist
the suburban districts witli its revenues, lam inclined
td think the* ratepayer's of the city will not entertain that
for it momertt. If they had a surplus they would want a
reduction id their own rate's. Therefore, it is the financial
difficulty which starids in the way, and until that can be
satisfactorily got dve'r I cannot see how this County
Courted is id We Wrdrtght about.-
This is exactly the spirit in which the deep
financial fossils o'f Australasia have tackled the
Federation question aUcl prevented it getting
further forward. And this soTt of attitude
will prevent either State or municipal Federa-
tion getting any further forward. It \Vill
always be “ premature.” There will never in
the next 45,000 centuries be a time when all the
municipalities around Sydney or Melbourne, or
any other place, will be in exactly the same
state of solvency, and have exactly the same pro-
portion of debt or taxation dr assets or anything
else. It will always be the case so long as
the world lasts that if Sydney amalgamates
with Five Dock, and either of them can’t pay
its way on a rate of Is. 4d. in the £, the rate
\4ill have to be' raised. And if this is a reason
why the 40 municipalities around Sydney
shouldn’t amalgamate, it is all equally good
reason why Sydney should split into 40 more
municipalities, for if George-street can get along
with a rate of Is. in the £ it is manifestly un-
just that it should be taxed more heavily to
make up for the poverty of Mary Ann-street,
Ultimo. Hardly one of the wearisome aider-
men seems to take any account of the fact that
amalgamation w’ould largely reduce working
expenses, and that the saving in this direction
should more than make up for all losses. A ten-
tency to go down on all fours and search for a
farthing with a microscope pervades the whole
business, and the general aldermanic idea seems
to be that the entire scheme must stand still
until that farthing is found. Which further
confirms The Bulletin in its low opinion of
aldermen in general.
“Hugh Boyle ” to The Bulletin :
Regarding the question of illegitimate births in N.S.
Wales, touched on in a recent issue, I would like to draw
your attention to the following figures. The increase
shown therein should be capable of explanation :
These figures are correct, taken from the Statistical Re-
gister. Is it only a coincidence that since 1886, the earli-
est year in which one could expect the Public Instruction
Act of 1880 to have any noticeable effect on these figures,
the proportion of illegitimate births has, both in town
and country, from year to year increased? It is a very
interesting and very important problem, and I should like
to know what you, or maybe some of your contributors,
may have to say oil the matter.
Chief Justice Madden turned up at a Mel-
bourne church congress last week to defend the
Vic. Bench from a charge of heathenism hurled
by that super-righteous critic, parson Fitchett.
Mr. Madden on the religious lay was glib and
interesting as usual, and as usual he gave him-
self away with characteristic Irish enthusiasm.
He talked of the soul’s longing after immor-
tality (for it would be terrible to entertain the
thought of a Madden terminating in ashes and
dust), also of the decadence of nations through
want of faith, and the dreadful lesson of the
French Revolution. Then he dealt judicially
with “ the evidence of the public economist to
the effect that if a State is to succeed it must
be based on high purpose. When people can
see in successful chicanery anything but dis-
honor, their faces are set towards destruction,
and when this is allowed within the bounds of
any community, that community is looking to
the road leading to destruction.” Heavens
and earth ! John Madden, in the billets of
Supreme Court Judge and Acting-Governor, has
enjoyed particular opportunities for getting on
his hind legs to denounce successful chicanerists
and frown upon them in a sassiety way of
frowning. And how, deah friends, hath his
Honor employed these talents which the Lord
hath given him? Did the boomsters whom
Gov. Hopetoun had very properly cold-
shouldered come bouncing back to Government
’Ouse when Madden assumed the crown—or
didn’t they ? How has Judge Madden shaped
towards the nothing-in-the-£ insolvents, and
what are his notions of dishonor, anyhow ? A
good many people will accept his sermon as
evidence that his own cheerful face and waxed
moustache ate—little as he may suspect the
fact —set in the wrong direction. The circum-
stance' that he parts his back hair down the
middle, as though it were hair on the top of
his bald head, makes no difference. He looketh
towards destruction, notwithstanding,

Item from a recent early-closing meeting at
Sydney:
The Rev. Dili, Macky (Scots’ Church) said the' object of
the meeting was to promote a bill which had i?> view the
closing of shops at 6 o’clock each evening, excepting on
Wednesdays and Saturdays, with a half-holiday in the
middle of the week to compensate for the long hours on
Saturdays. His belief was that we lived too quickly. The
life of the average man was now 30 years, but he lived
more in those 30 years than did the patriarchs of old.
Some men, he knew, would not work. He had known 4s.
per day refused because the men said it was starvation
wages. For such men he would put work on one side
and raw hide on the other.
The Bulletin wonders if this good old hunky
bigot, who has actually known men refuse to
work for 245, a week, ever tried to live and
support a family on that wage himself. If he
hasn’t, then it suggests the formation of a
Society to make pastor Dill Macky live on 4s.
a day till further orders and see how he likes
it. This will be a beautiful world when the
Rev. Dill’s system comes in force. Thenevery
man who is out of work, and ean get work at
4s. a day, will have to take it, under penalty of
being flogged by the feeling apostle, and as
every boss will he able to get men at 4s. a day
no one will pay any more, and as a man at that
wage can’t put much in the plate Parson Dill
will probably have a hungry time. The re-
marks quoted, by the way, were made at a
meeting to promote the abolition of sweating,
and Parson Dill was on the platform as a
sympathiser with the sweated worker, and it
was in that capacity that he wanted to flog
people who wouldn’t work at 4s. a day. If this
is Scotch sympathy what is the other thing
like ?

To The Bulletin from a N.S. W. male school-
teacher !
1 am one of nearly 400 teachers whose schools were re-
cently lowered from 9th-elass to lOth-class on account of
lessened attendance,
I have over 10 years’ service and 111. A classification.
My salary was £125 nett (including wife’s allowance
for teaching sewing) s it is now £lO3, and £2O for resi-
dence.
I have six children of my own,
I have not lost one month through sickness in whole
term of service.
I have never absented myself from duty.
I have never had complaints or been reported to iny
knowledge.
I have several times refused political influence offered
me.
I have given food to 18 travellers in one day, and never
yet turned a man away. I’m not religious ; but I’ll have
to refer them to some benevolent society in future.
If I patch my coat and trousers 1 lose the respect of my
pupils, and when the Inspector comes I shall lose marks ;
but I soon will be compelled to patch.
Further, I hate Chinamen, yet I am afraid I cannot
deal cash with anyone else, and if I get into debt the
storekeeper can have me dismissed if I cannot pay.
If the small attendance could be avoided by the teacher,
I should say serve him right: it can’t.
Does it seem just to you that after seven years’ service
(with present classification) I should he reduced to my
original salary ?
Is there not something wrong with the system which
allots such a fate to the bush-teachers, who do much of
the Dept’s hardest work under the most unpleasant con-
ditions, while single girls with no classification and not
half the service get as much salary in the cities V
Magistrate Panton, in passing some gilt-
edged remarks on the City of Melbourne Bank
business, post-dated the land-boom troubles a
few years. “ These overdrafts went on and
continued to increase,” said Panton gently,
“ until there passed over Melbourne that finan-
cial storm which succeeded to the period known
as the land-boom. Then the management real-
ised the loss, and set to work to provide for it.”
As a matter of fact the boom burst in 1889, and
during the years of waiting for the thunder-
storm to arrive every bank realised its loss and
knew that it was adding overdraft to overdraft
in order to conceal bad debts and avert a catas-
trophe. The general prayer of managers and
directors was, “Not in our time, O Lord!”
These elderly gents wanted to give themselves
a fair chance of death before dishonor, and it
says much for the tough consciences of sundry
Melbourne financiers that their banks haven’t
outlived them already. The C. of M. died
some time back, but not before Longmuik had
kindly left his corpse for the benefit of the
Board,

The Vic. Government seems to have made up
its mind to start building narrow-gauge lines
in the wilds of Gippsland. They are cheap,
which is a recommendation in the present state
of affairs ; and by adopting this sad and shoddy
expedient it is possible to get a lot of railway
for very little money, and thus open up the
districts which were so long neglected while
boom-lines were being heaped on top of each
other in the central counties. At the same
time, Vie, will almost certainly be very sorry
for the new departure by-and-bye. The narrow-
gauge line means the construction of special
rolling-stock. The rolling-stock is useful on the
narrow-gauge lines only; if some of it isn’t work-
ing and there is a press of business elsewhere
it can’t be shifted from one locality to another.
It can’t even be shifted from one narrow-gauge
line to another, if the only means of getting
there is over a section of the main line. It has
to be repaired in its own special district. The
goods carried by it have to be transferred with
great trouble and expense before they can
roach Melbourne, or any other large city.
There are inventions for minimising the trouble
involved, but even the best of them are laborious
and awkward. Moreover, the districts which
now swear that a narrow-gauge line will satisfy
them for many years to come only do so'
because they have to say something like
that to get their line at all. When they get
it they will almost certainly agitate and make*
their members agitate till the narrow-gauge
delusion is made a part of the general railway
system, and the expense of widening cuttings,
embankments, bridges, and tunnels, laying new
fails and sleepers, altering platforms, &c., will
make the proposed economy pan out very
badly. No part of Victoria will put up any
longer than- it can help with a worse railway
system than the rest of the province enjoys.
And in any case Australia has too many diverse
gauges already, and to add one more to the list
only makes the present very bad confusion a
shade-worse.
It seems a waste of time to talk of demo-
cratising the Vic. Leg. Council. Thirteen re-
tiring members were returned unopposed to
their" Fat Man paradise last week, and the eon-
test for a fourteenth seat is merely owing to
the fact that the late occupant doesn’t want to
go back to it. Victoria refuses to trouble
about the constitution of the Hupper 'Ouse.
Once inside, the most undesirable of legislators
can stay there on his demerits as long as he
chooses. The poor little proposal to oust Bar-
good collapsed for want of nourishment, and
the soft-goods deity was numbered among the
illustrious 18 aforesaid, together -with such
hardy annuals as Robert Reid and James
Balfour, whose fitness for dealing with amend-
ment of the Companies Act comes under the
head of “ questionable.” The country that
sends an ex-director of the Freehold Invest-
ment Co. to vote upon the matter of paying
divs. from paper profits certainly deserves to-
be misgoverned. Last week’s list of unopposed
councillors is sad, sad reading, brightened by
only one ray of hope. Thirteen is the unlucky
number, and the councillor doomed to die
within the year is bound to leave a vacancy.
Perchance the one who passes hence may have
a respectable successor.

TheN.S. Wales Civil Service Superannuation
Fund is growing more alarmingly insolvent
every day. The pensioners keep drawing their
pensions, and the old public servants who will
shortly become pensioners keep on paying, but
the young civil servants have retired almost
unanimously under the new regulations. There-
fore, the present and immediately prospective
claims haven’t diminished, while the revenue
out of which they are paid has gone down
seriously. Moreover, it is part of the bargain
under the new Act that the C.S. who stops con-
tributing is to receive back all his payments
with 3 per cent, compound interest when he
leaven the service. The reasonable course would
have been to let him have his money back at
once without interest, so that lie could have a
drunk with it to celebrate the reduction in hie
screw—the prospect of getting, say, £lB 10s.,
in 35 years or so, isn’t much to live for ; but in
that ease the claims would have shattered the
fund on the spot, so the evil day was post-
poned. The money to meet these refunds
hasn’t been put aside, so it is going to pay pen-
sions, and when it is wanted it will probably be
very much absent. Meanwhile, the idea of
largely reducing the pensions now being paid—
i.e., of Repudiation—is under serious considera-
tion. Many of the pensioners are too old to kick
much, and it seems an easy way of postponing
the difficulty a little longer. A large batch of
recently-retrenched civil servants have also to
be “ compensated ” this year, and if this com-
pensation comes out of the revenue a big deficit
is imminent. On the other hand, if it comes
out of the Superannuation Fund that institu-
tion might as well give up the ghost on the spot.
Dear Bulletin,— -Stand a little more “ meejuni
of exchange ” —from an avow r ed bimetallist
this time ? The fact is, we struck a snag, an
awkward one. Not all of us are Attorneys-
General, able to afford investigation trips to
Japan ; and some of us who can and do afford
it strictly limit our investigations while we are
there to other “ meejurns.” Most of us have,
therefore, to accept others’ words, especially
about gold and silver. Now, the whole point
in the yellow man plus white money versus
white man plus yellow money controversy, or
struggle, or whatever it is, is this : Now that
silver has fallen, does the yellow man demand
more silver coins? All bimetallist warnings are
based on the assumption that he doesn’t. The
common argument is something like this: —
“Coal from Japan used to lie sold at foreign
ports for (say) a sovereign a ton. For that the
merchant got (say) 4.V yen (silver money). Now
the merchant gets 7 or 8 yen, on account of the
fall in the exchange value of silver. But as the
purchasing value of silver in silver-using coun-
tries has not fallen, he pays away no more
yen than he did. He can, therefore, now
afford, if necessary, to sell his coal for
3 or 4 yen per ton less than he could before
silver fell, or, say, for 12s. 6d. per ton.
Thus” triumphantly “the yellow man
with the white money is,” &c. But now from
the East come some awkward figures tabulated
by the Japanese Monetary Investigations Com-
mittee. I don’t know the committee; it may
be a council of lunatics : but, anyway, it
gets its figures printed by good papers. And
if it lies, it lies with circumstance. The figures,
built on the lines of Sauerbeck’s index num-
bers, indicate the cost of living at Osaka and
Tokio during each year from 1873 to 1894.
Rice, the chief article of food, went from 100 in
the first year to 165 in the last. That is about
the average. Assuming the Jap. to be much
like the European or Australian in this respect,
no reliance is placed on comparative tables
showing the rise in wages ; but it is certain
that if there has been this advance in cost of
living, there has been (as is claimed) an almost
corresponding rise in wages. In that case—
well, though the principles of bi-metallism arc
not seriously affected, the most effective rally-
ing-cry to the poor white must hereafter have a
hollow sound ; and, after all, if it’s true, the
Newcastle coal-miner isn’t necessarily so near
extermination as some people have been care-
fully explaining to him that he is. Anyway,
the attention of Moreton Frewen ought to be
called to these figures ; for he laid it down as

Ed. Bulletin, — Re Australian Universities. It
must be conceded in all frankness that Uni'
versify men have added nothing to our litera'
ture. We have no literature : one work of
fiction of almost second-rate greatness, and
certain short sweet lilts (whose motive has been
the novelty of nature here and whose incident
that of our social life) constitute our literature.
Nor can we have one till there is some great
life-struggle for nationhood. Our original
scientific work, too, has been fragmentary ; and
so it will remain until our Universities are made
to approximate more nearly to the continental
ones, especially to those of Germany. When
they are sufficiently well endowed to give life-
fellowships to distinguished observers, we shall
have good original work. Meanwhile, when a
man completes his course, he must turn into the
world and make a living. The weakness of our
Universities is that they are mere technical
schools for qualifying doctors, engineers,
teachers and lawyers ; that they have departed
and are departing from traditional models.
What will they become when they are put
more directly under the thumb of some illiter-
ate, fetich-worshipping Minister of Education ?
'V hat if the present Education officials (pro-
moted how ? and W'hen ?) had the exasperating
and cramping power of dictating to the Pro-
fessors of Philosophy and History their attitude
on parts of their curricula ? One word more.
There is something charming in the irony of
your correspondent Pvella in referring to the
“stimulating teaching” of the Sydney Pro-
fessors of Law and Philosophy ! He even
brackets them with Badham !—O.H.
The awful bunglesomeness of Protectionist-
leader Lyne was again exemplified in the debate
over M'Gowen’s proposal to take a popular
vote on the fiscal question. Premier Reid
offered to take the vote at next general election ;
M‘Go wen concurred; Lyne, who should have
grabbed at the suggestion with hands and feet
and teeth, and pinned Reid down to his pro-
posal on the spot, demurred. The two years
would have given the Free-raid party time to
hang itself comfortably. It would have given
time for it to show a big deficit, whereas now
it would go to the country on the strength of a
bogus surplus produced by a bogus cash basis.
It would have allowed N.S.W. to try a spell of
something resembling real Freetrade, which it
has never yet experienced, and see what the
tiling is like ; and given it time to forget what
a feeble apology for Protection the Lyne crowd
gave it when they were in power. It would
allow of the publication of two more issues of
the province’s statistics, and in them there
ought to be some good material for Lyne
and Go. to throw at the heads of unbelievers.
Inside two years the public will have leisure to
see whether the land-tax is the devastating
thing Lyne says it is, and to be disgusted at
the income-tax. By that time Victoria will
probably have recovered from the land-boom
smash, and will no longer serve the Free-
raiders as a “ dreadful example,” whose ill-luck
can be all put down to Protection. Another
bank should have gone broke by that time —The
Bulletin declines to mention which bank it is ;
and the N.S.W. sugar-industry ought to be
about dead; and the N.S. W. Benevolent Asylum
will have two more reports out ; and the people
who voted for Reid won’t be “ jolly and fat ; ”
and the Protectionist party may have a Sydney
morning paper of its own ; and many other
things that are now chaotic should have got
into order. If Lyne has faith in himself and
his creed this is the best time to do a lot of
peaceful waiting that can be imagined.

A Parkes story, re-dished by Patchett
Martin When Parkes was a working man
m Sydney, he was going home one Satur-
day night with the poor sum of ten shil-
lings in his pocket for his wife’s house-
hold expenses. But he chanced to see in a
second-hand bookseller’s window a book of William
Cobbett’s ticketed 10s., and at once entered the
shop, bought the book, and returned home penni-
less. . Not only did he commit this wild extrava-
gance, but in after years he boasted of it ! ”

IN MEMORIAM.
Harrison. In remembranceof James Harrison
who passed away suddenly in his cottage on Point
Henry, Oono Bay, 3rd Sept., 1893 ; aged 77 years.
He was the first man in the whole world to make
ice by artificial process on a commercial scale for
commercial uses. The invention pioneered the
way for a» the existing oversea traffic in perish-
able products. The inventor’s reward was finan-
cial rum. The world reaps the benefit. The man
himseif ,s by the world forgot. He sleeps in the
Geelong Cemetery, not a stone to mark the grave
Inserted by his only surviving brother, Daniel
on > Carolme-street, South Yarra. Mel-
The Scapegoat.
(Another view of the Bank Manager who is Dead.)
The man who’s dead—
Whose spirit fled
Before the bank went bung—
He bears the blame;
His evil fame
Reluctantly is sung
By live directors, one and all;
’Twas he who brought about the fall
By doing deeds beyond recall—
The man who’s dead.
The man’s who’s dead-,
He lost his head ;
He certainly went daft.
To pals in want
He’d gaily grant
A sinful overdraft.
The good directors did not know
Where all the money used to go-,
They put such faith in So-ancl-So
The man who’s dead.
No need to dread
The man who’s dead ;
He couldn’t breathe a word,
E’en though he’d fain
To-day explain
How certain things occurred.
Some sorrow for his fate wo feel—
Condemned for ever to conceal
The simple truths he might reveal
Were he not dead.
The man who’s dead
In narrow bed
Lies safe from mortal care ;
His sunken chest
Is not depressed
By what he has to bear.
Though bitterly directors rail
Against the victim of their tale,
No law can run him into gaol,
The man who’s dead.
The Wild-Cat Column.
A proposal for the reconstruction of the Queens-
land National Bank, which is gravely propounded by one
of its ex-officers, deserves the palm as being absolutely
the cheekiest thing yet mentioned in the reconstruction
line. In the first place, the other two Queensland banks
—the Itoj’al and the N.Q. —are to amalgamate with the
Q.N. They both have a good reputation for soundness,
and they have nearly five times as large a capital in pro-
portion to their liabilities as the Q.N. ; but it is suggested
that they should become responsible for that institution’s
liabilities, taking things just as they are. Then the Go-
vernment is to take £5,000,000 worth of shares, and pay
up £2,500,000 in cash ; so that the whole concern will
have a nominal capital of £10,000,000, of which £7,761,000
will be subscribed and £3,830,000 paid up—two-thirds of
it by Government. The Royal and the N.Q. would put
into the pool between them £610,000 of shareholders’
inoney and about £1,060,000 of liabilities; the Q.N.B.
would put in about £070,000 of shareholders’ money and
over £9,000-,000 Of liabilities. If half the advances of
the two smaller banks went bad and paid nothing
in the £ they could still meet all liabilities without
calling up fresh capital; if less than one-fifth of the Q.N.’s
advances went wrong it would be broke—vet the three
are (according to this authorised or unauthorised scheme)
to be _ lumped together on the same footing. The
State is to put up 21 millions and make itself liable for
another 21 millions, and it is not apparently to have
any preferential claim ; it is simply to come into the
hideously depreciated institution and give par value
for shares that are at more than 80 per cent, dis-
count. The other two banks are to come in at the
nominal value of their shares, regardless of the fact
that they are worth about 40 per cent, more than the
Q.N.’s shares on the open market. Also the new bank is to
be the sole note-issuing institution of the province, and
nothing is said about the State, which is to supply two-
thirds of the money, having any commanding voice in the
management. Also there is no suggestion about any
inquiry before the State comes in. It would be interesting
to know who inspired this amazing proposition, and if it
is really the small thing that the Q.N.B. is asking the
Government to do for it. The Kanakaland Bank’s capa-
city for asking small things is pretty well-known.
* * * *
The West Australian Bank announces that the
half-year’s profit up to 30th June is £12,284 against
£10,972 last half and £10,315 the one before, so that
things apparently continue to look up. The usual divi-
dend of 17 Yi per cent, per annum absorbs £7OOO and the
balance is carried forward. By way of grabbing the boom
by the forelock, this institution has issued 2000 more £lO
shares at a premium of £l2 10s., so the capital goes up
from £BO,OOO to £lOO,OOO, and the reserve fund from
£lOO,OOO to £125 000. The deposits have increased from
£1,326,000 to £1 V>S,OOO, which is a stupendous rise inside
six months, and s«vo-thirds of them bear no interest. The
bank has £929,000 cash on hand—it seemingly lives by
keeping the cash on hand and gloating over it - and its
advances are only £790,000. The quaint features about
the W.A. Bank are that it has a very small capital in pro-
portion to its deposits ; that it pays hardly any interest
on deposits \ that it lends out very little 11101163'' —the coin
it keeps to look at far exceeds all its advances ; and that
it is too conservative even to invest its money in State
securities, of which it only holds £20,000 worth. Its way
of doing business was well suited to the holy calm of old-
tune Westralia. In the howling’, stormy east no bank
with £lOO,OOO capital would be likely to get nearly
£1,900,000 of deposits, most of them free of interest, so
that it could pay huge dividends merely by lending out
half the depositors’ money and sitting on the rest. There
was a time when things were like that in the east, but it is
gone. If the W.A. Bank doesn’t stake its own immortal
sou on the boom, however, and doesn’t go booming
itself so as to be wrecked when the backwash comes, it
should have a great time while the bubble lasts.

Apropos a recent Bulletin par. about lost notes
as a source of revenue. The Bank of Van Diemen’s Land
which broke in 1892, has still 1389 worth of notes out-
standing, and as only £3 worth were presented for pay-
ment durm;' the year, it is probable that the balance will
mostly never be heard of again. The Bank of V.D.L.
lived 03 years, and its highest note-circulation was
about 130,000. The prolit from note-leakage certainly
but still it is one source of revenue for the
.btate Bank to reckon with.

to pay the stupendous dividend of 35} per cent, it divides
among its shareholders £17,974, or £916 more than its

dropped in one year from £5027 to £3977, which looks as
if some of the securities were rather less healthy than thev
were. The Victoria is a solid concern, with £50,000 capital
and £BO,OOO of reserves, but even a very solid concern is
not well advised when it rushes quite so violently as all
this after a .35)4 per cent, dividend, in the face of a large
reduction in profits. °

* * * *
From Ist January to 22nd August, ’OO, the im-
ports at Sydney amounted to £10,424,466 and exports to
only £7,227,575. The Foreign-trade theory is that im-
ports are naturally and invariably paid for by exports.
These figures seem to show that imports can be partly
paid for by living on our accumulated capital or bv going
m debt, and pawning the country still more deeply to the
foreign capitalist. If imports were always paid for by
exports, then a country couldn’t get into debt.

G-oodlet and Smith, the Sydney firm whose

compete with low-wage competitors abroad. The policy
which was to make the community jolly and fat once
more.
* * * *
The Maoriland Midland Railway Co. has left a
number of its land-buyers in the lurch by the good old
time-payment swindle which has become so familiar of
late years. The co. obtained its land-grant by instal-
ments as it pottered along with its railway, and sold the
land to farmers on long terms. When a man bought £5OO
worth of land and paid £5O cash he was required to give a
mortgage for the balance of £450, and this document re-
mained in existence until the last instalment was paid.
The English co. pawned these mortgages, and went on
collecting the instalments, and seemingly applied them,
not towards paying otf the mortgages, but to its own pur-
poses. Consequently, the buyer finds that the whole £450
is still due, and he is no nearer getting his title than he
was before. And as the co. is broke, and the ruins of it
are at the other end of the globe, no redress is obtainable
from it and nobody' seems to be responsible. The Briti h
syndicate has more ways of being a bowelless fri.u 1
than any other institution Tiie Bulletin knows of.

Castlemaine (Vic.) Brewery Cl, in the boom-
time of 1888, wrote up the book-value of its properties
£59,.090, and thereby ran its reserve up to £BO,OOO. Now.
it writes them down £65,000, and the reserve gets back (o
£15,000. The story of the boom in a nutshell.
