Sargood, on the Companies Act Amendment
Bill, had the cast-iron effrontery to say that though Vic-
toria is far behind the “old country” in company law,
the O.C. has “attained a pre-eminence in rascality not
yet reached here.” A statement of that sort, made in
Parliament for publication by the local press, ought to he
a punishable offence. Sargood can’t name any modern
instance of British financial rascality', barring the Jabez
Balfour, to compare with the unpunished crimes of Smell-
bourne. And J.B. is doing his 14 years. Jfe has to pay
for his rascality, whereas the Melbourne villains have
swindled gratis.
* * * *
The travesty of justice by which a whilom
J.P. was acquitted by direction of the Judge on a charge
of alleged fraudulent conduct as an agent or bailee, has
almost roused the Victorians to take a languid interest in
the state of the law which pots a dishonest “servant” but
daren’t try an alleged fraudulent “agent” or “bailee.”
The position appears to be this : A authorises B to pay
money to O : O collects the money and keeps it : the
Victorian Crimes Act knows no reason why he should not
keep it. If C bad been a “servant” of A he would have
been guilty of felony. But an “ agent ”is too respectable
to be made a felon.

able as an epic poet, or an Isaac Newton.

£lOO if you join me as half-and-half partner.—Yours

* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —“To-day is the day of big
things.” “Big” industry (in French phrase) has wiped
out little industry; the big mutual store or limited lia-
bility co. of 700 or 1000 shareholders has eaten up.and
altogether annihilated the individual trader. When a
big store or large manufacturing co. starts this canni-
balistic process is there any talk of compensation ? Yet if
a majority of the community start a co-operative concern
of any sort through their officers, the Government of the
day, there is forthwith heard through the length and
breadth of the land a howl for compensation—Compensa-
tion ! Did the howlers compensate the small private
firm or individual trader whom they wdped out ?—B.
•** ' •
The extension of the Melbourne railway system
from Spencer-street to Princes Bridge has been a curse to
certain Melb. property-owners. It was anticipated by
everyone that that portion of the city from the corner of
Bourke and Spencer streets and right along Flinders-
street to Elizabeth-street would be almost fabulously
increased in value by the aforesaid extension. The
reverse has happened, and as far as hotel properties are
concerned the expected improvement has blasted the ex-
pectations of those unfortunate tenants along the thor-
oughfare who leased the premises at boom-price rentals.
Hardly one of the eight or ten big pubs, along the line
can show from week to week even what might be called
a book-keeping profit.

W. Alison, Vice-President of Sydney Bimetallic
League, debits this against monometallism and the appre-
ciation of the present “ meejum ” :—“ In N.S.W., at 1873
prices, 100,000 bales of wool would have paid the interest
on our present public debt; it now takes 200,000 at pre-
sent prices.”

A Charleville butcher lately sold a hide in Bris-
bane for £2 14s. It weighed no less than 911 b. A caution,
isn’t it, when the hide fetches more than the carcase ! If
this sort of thing continues, Queensland will drop the
cattle-industry and take to raising hides alone.
* * * *
The Broken Hill mines have so far, this year,
rather more than maintained their record of one fatal
accident per div. During the first seven months of the
year there were 10 fatal accidents, 12 serious but not fatal,
and 21 less serious, but bad enough to be reported to the
Mines Dept. Meantime, there is persistent cutting in
contract-prices, with the result that the majority of old
v’orkmen who are not absolutely tied to the Hill are
following their bosses to the West, and new 7 and often raw-
hands are replacing them.
Old and practical miners, who are themselves
small shareholders, complain unceasingly, when they can
do so in confidence, of the waste of ore and of timber by
present methods. Men are paid by the foot or the “set”
—all they have to see is that “ the tape stretches ” so far.
No great care is therefore taken to prevent the ore from
becoming mixed with the mullock ; and thousands of ton 3
of good stuff are thus thrown aside and used for filling up.
Then, since the object is to get the ore, &c., away any-
where—it doesn’t matter to them where —extravagantly
heavy charges are put in, and the material is shot through
the bottom timbers, crushing and splintering scores of
them every day. Again, suppose a drive or a stope is
following airing near the wall, leaving two or three feet
between the outside timbers and this casing. Now,
though this portion of the lode is generally the richest,
it is invariable left by the contractors. But if the miners
were paid per ton, just as coal-miners are, not only would
every pound of this ore be taken out, but greater care
would be ordinarily exercised in the working faces.
Metallurgist Savage, who was Schlapp’s under-
study at the B.H. Proprietary, leaves the big co’s employ
to take charge of the same branch at the Ashcroft sulphide
works, Newcastle.
The chairman of the Yuba resigns. The first
out.
The large volume of water which is draining
into the deep level of the Junction, though delaying
mining operations, is taken to be a very good sign. (Most
things in mines and mining, by the way, are taken to be
very good signs.) The ore from which such large quanti-
ties of water drains must, it is thought, be “ spongy ” ;
and it is this stuff which, when found in the sulphide
zone, generally contains the lowest percentage of the
worse than unprofitable zinc. It reads well for the Junc-
tion North, and points to the probability of better patches
of stuff being found in the North than have so far been
worked.
A sensational fall of ro.of happened on Monday
week at the Wickham and Bullock Island Colliery (New-
castle). From Friday, 21st, to Monday, 24th, bumping
bad been heard at irregular intervals in the district where
the fall took place, and the men working there were
withdrawn. The mine is 23Gft. deep in this section ; the
seam (with bands) 21ft. 6in., and the consequent enor-
mous dislocation of the superincumbent strata is evi-
denced by extensive surface fissures traversing the public
roads and adjacent swamps. These swamps are covered
at high-water, and the miners naturally fear that the
tidal waters may percolate through and invade the work-
ings. This opinion is not shared by the manager, who
relies on the 100 ft. of grey post rock immediately abov'e
the coal and another 9ft. band of strorig blue clay to check
any inrush of water. The immense mass of disturbed
cover is still grinding and crushing ori the solid Byd.
pillars, from the face of Which the varying pressure is
forcing large flakes and lumps. So much cpal has
been shivered off in this way that it will need
two shifts of 200 men to clear the roadways. The Govt,
inspector refuses to state whether the mine is safe or riot,
but should the men desire to resume Work will go dowri
with them and advise. The men Want to leave the mine,
but the management, viewing with alarm their desertion
at a time of scarcity of skilled labor, declines to allow
them to remove their tools, and insists ori the usual
14 days’ notice. They threaten to proceed agairist the
nieri, but this is palpablj 7 mere bluff, for it is idle to main-
tain either that men cari be forced to Work under condi-
tions of prima-facie danger or be made to suffer for
their refusal. The workings of this mine extend under
the Waters of the harbor, and so shallow is it (at orie
part) that the miners at work can hear the pulse of pass-
ing steamers, the rattle of chains, and the thud of falling
anchors.
* * * *
Highland Mary (Swamp Oak) N.5.W.—147 tons
for 5800 z., and another 200 oz. at least in pyrites and tail-
ings. The vein averages 18in., worked to a depth of 200 ft.
and over 300 ft. in length ; country rock, a Gympie-like
black slate, with bars of diorite outside. This, like many
another good mine, has paid its way from the jump ; the
only capital required was the rent, and that because it is
payable in advance. Owned by the Brown Bros., who
know a mine and how to work it when they find a good
one.
* * * *
Grenfell, N.S.W. : —“The sal* of the Young
O’Brien gold-mine to an English co. has been completed.”
Money not stated. A good prospect at a moderate price.
Vein nearly three feet; length of ore chute about 200 ft. ;
worked to a depth of 300 ft., and little or no development
work below that level. Value of ore, under the oz. and
more than half; most of the gold is carried in a four to
six-inch streak running in the centre of the vein.

As more than onee predicted by the writer, the
Cobar boom is almost played out. Says a local paper:
“ Wild cats, like chickens, come home to roost. Cobar
has been overdone, or, rather, done to death by boomers
and sharks, and most of our boomed stocks stink in the
nostrils of speculators.” Most of these “ cats ” are in the
locality of Barrass and Conley's wonderful property, and
the fact that B. and C. found the gold on a 250-acre selection
which they bought (besides taking up several outside
leases) was somehow or other omitted from each pro-
spectus ; also the fact that that property itself has not
been sufficiently developed to say whether it is going to
be as big as it looks.
* * * *
Oobar : “ There has been a fall in Mt. Pleasant
shares.” And no wonder. Last crushing it was 900 z.,
“from a rough clean-up,” this time only 500 z., and noth-
ing about the roughness of the clean-up. “ Better re-
turns are expected.” “ Worse returns ” seem never to be
expected. The sooner Cobarites admit and act on the
unmistakeable truth that their mines are too low-grade to
pay more than wages when worked with 10-head batteries
without cyanide, and take the Occidental as an example
of it, the better for the camp.
* * * *
Cobar : “Specimens taken from the Queen of
the Earth mine, Coolgardie, are on view here. They are
wonderfully rich.” Specimens as rich as these are never
found at Cobar —which is a very good thing for the dis-
trict, if people only knew it.

through in one ?
* * * *
The rainfall in Coolgardie district during ’95
(the only year for which there is a complete record, and
which is believed to have been an exceptionally wet year)
was only 6'79in. It cannot be safely assumed that the
average rainfall would exceed sin. The average at
Southern Cross for the last two years has been s’26in.
* * * *
It seems pretty plain that at the best the price
of water at Coolgardie under the proposed Govt, scheme
(which will take at least three and possibly four years to
carry out) will be between 3s. 6d. and ss. per lOOOgals.
Compare this with Bendigo, where the Govt, supplies
batteries at 2id. per lOOOgals., and ask when are they
going to have "cheap mining on Coolgardie ?
* * * *
If the Financial Times is to be believed, Prof.
Nicholas has written a great report on the Gladiators’
mine in the Black Flag district, W.A. “He now states
that he has several thousand tons of ore which will give
lOoz. to the ton, and that the reef is now exposed for a
length of 1000 ft. As £40,000 worth of ore is now actually
in sight, it is clear that he is not reporting haphazard,
and "that it only requires the completion of the winding
machinery and crushing plant to give definite and perma-
nent results.”

The block at Fremantle (W.A.) grows worse
and worse, and steamers from the East now race for berths,
sometimes passing Albany and carrying with them cargo
consigned to that port.
* * » »
No sane person would take serious notice of
mine-puffs printed in Westralian papers run by com-
panies consisting of people interested in the different
local mines. It often happens that when, according to
the local Archimedean lever, an alleged mine has fiOOO
tons of 4oz. stone at grass, the unprejudiced stranger can
on inspection find no indications of any metal whatever,
save some rusty jam-tins.
* ‘ * * *
United Scotchman, Norseman line of reef, has
crushed 1000 tons for 1200 oz. Shares, 12s. before the
crushing, but quickly slumped. Claim (writes a Bulletin
correspondent) is badly pegged—the cap of the reef being
three chains from their east boundary. The reef, under-
lying 45deg. to the east, at about 200 ft. will be in Dr.
Simon’s co.’s claim.
* * * *
The saddest of sad sights in Westralia is the
number of English companies working “mines” on salt-
bush flats, miles away from any known lode or auriferous
belt, with no possible chance of ever finding a reef, much
less gold. J. Bullion is cheerfully finding the coin to keep
up this ghastly farce. Main shafts are going down, and
the most modern and costly mining machinery going up.
The managers, who have to keep writing reports, have a
sick time, and the men in the “ mines” and the engineers
0 n the plants take no pride in their work. Some have
aeen men enough to walk off, to work on such jobs being
more than they could stand. One day, when the boom
is dead, and the money all spent, some local newspapers
will assert their independence by publishing a list of these
salt-bush “cats.”
* * • *
London Financial Bulletin (which, judged
from a recent leading article, appears to be drunk most of
its time) shrieks over the success of the “ Ore Auto-
matic reduction process.” The secrets of the process are
not revealed, but it is gently whispered that “ heat, and
the action of certain combined gases on the ore, effect the
disintegrating process.” The hardest quartz is reduced
to sand of the finest powder in from 20 mill, to an hour,
and, said the chairman df the W.A. Trading Syndicate;
vVhich has secured the patent rights, “ the extraordinary
thing is, as rie found froni careful experiment, that if the
ore fails to break into particles within an hour, it is proof
positive that it contains no metal.” The process appears
to be an infringement of one being worked out by the
writer, that riot only declines to waste its valuable time in
breaking up metalless ores, but indignantly Chucks them
Out arid hits the feeder iri the eye.

Boomster Alick Forrest having been to London
and formed a big bullion co., now, as a legislator, moves
to amend the W.A. Gold Fields Act to provide that, for
the first twelve months after the approval of an applica-
tion for a gold lease, not less than two men should be
employed on the lease, after which the present labor-con-
ditions should come in force. How disinterested! Two
men for the first year ! 100 acres manned by eight men !
How very lovely for the boomer!
* » * *
There is but one paper in London, so far as the
writer is aware, which has the audacity, or independence,
to receive prospectuses, and in the same issue condemn
the schemes as unworthy of public investment. Last
year it foreshadowed the downfall of Bhodes and Bar-
nato. It has established itself by its simple respect for
truth. It writes of W.A. mines thus: “One of these
days it may be possible to deal in Westralians on a square
sort of basis, knowing what one is doing and what one is
getting for one’s money. But that time is not yet. With
very few exceptions, the Westralian mines are unproved,
and their value, if any, unknown. To deal in them is
simply to deal in gambling-counters, and to back any of
the numerous offspring of Hannan’s and other prolific
company-producing districts is gambling with much
longer odds against one, and under far less"agreeable con-
ditions than to back a number at the infantile sport of
petits chevaux, so popular in continental casinos.”
* * * *
London shareholders in Bay ley’s are disgusted.
They held a meeting at the end of June, at which it was
observed that very soon after tens of thousands of shares
were placed in London regular divs. suddenly ceased with-
out any explanation from the directors ; and it was deter-
mined that the gold in the tailings should be exhausted
before consent to reconstruction was given—and not then
until the details of the scheme were properly placed before
them. Poor Bull, how he must appreciate the Australian
press !

The promised Victorian legislation for ventila-
tion in mines will not come a day too soon. Dr. Eadie,
Bendigo health officer, speaking" of the deaths from
phthisis in the district, says that the rate will increase
until such time as adequate ventilation in mines is made
compulsory by Parliament.
* * * *
The permanency and regularity of Collins’reef,
worked so successfully by the Long Tunnel and Extended
mines at Wa’.halla, are almost without parallel in Aus-
tralasia. It has gone from the surface to a depth of
1722 ft. without a break. The monthly yield of each mine
averages about 2100 oz. The Tunnel Co. has produced to
date about 610,0000 z. (say 25 tons of gold, value
£2,128,090); paid in divs. £1,144,200, equal to £467 per
share. Nothing has been spent to prospect for other
parallel lodes probably running through its leases. Com-
pared with the Extended mine, it appears to be expen-
sively worked. It returns the investor, at the present
market-rate, 13 per cent., while the Extended returns 324
per cent. The Tunnel Co.’s cost for firewood amounts to
nearly £lO,OOO a year, or equal to £54 per horse-power,
yet it rejects Karlbaum’s electric scheme, whereby he
proposes to utilise the water of the Aberfeldy and Thomson
rivers to work all the mines and light the town, and offers
the motive-power at £24 per h.p.
* * * *
The difficulty of selling small parcels of gold at
a fair figure is one of the woes of far-out Vic. diggers.
In one district the writer knows of small parties who have
been selling for years at £3 10s. and £3 12s. 6d. an ounce,
and have just found out that the metal is worth £3 18s. fid.
of anybody’s money. Local storekeepers are usually the
offenders.

* * * *
An old-time Bulletin correspondent“ I have
travelled over 1300 miles since leaving the Central, visit-
ing the Cape, Towers, Percy, Finnigan’s Big Beef, George-
town-, Cumberland and, lastly, the new rush at Green-
hills, located about 24 miles N.W. of Georgetown and A
very likely place, tt is hard to form a correct estimate of
the gold got so far, but for sure there have been 4000 odd
ozs. accounted for. While I was there one specimen was
unearthed—ls6oz., which dollied 920 z. gold. There are
two classes of gold—waterworh slugs arid quartz speci-
mens, the gold being different value. It is scattered
over a mile square, and is got on the surface as
well as in the wash. The bottom is rotten granite,
the soil above red loam. There is no line to
go by, and, as a rule, the gullies contain little gold, the
heavy stuff being got on the rises, away from them. It is
the sort of a field on which, so far, newchums have done
better than old diggers. Some have done well, and others
average wages. The nearest permanent water is six miles
off (Gilbert Biver), but they have got water (shallow) by
sinking. They are called the spoonbill miners owing to
the custom of going about the surface turning over the
stones with a spoon-shaped piece of wood, ‘ slug-hunting.’
When the wet season comes there is bound to be a fair
rush there. There are any number of reefs showing a
little gold on the top, but, so far, none have been touched.
All the Etheridge fields, right to Georgetown, are dead to
the world, with the exception of ‘ Finnigan’s,’ The Cum-
berland and Durham are simply dead. The dogs bark at
you in the town when you arrive, and the goats seem to
have possession of most of the humpies. Croydon is the
most improved town I have seen (been away four years),
both as regards the mines and the district generally.
Business is sound, and the wild old drinking-days have
gone and given place to hard work. Many of the erstwhile
deserted outside camps have been manned and are carrying
a good population. Dozens of small shows are paying the
working-miner well that would not be looked at four years
ago ; and the average tributors are doing well. Many old
miners have returned from Westralia, Croydon being, in
their opinion, better for them.”

battery, got no less than 25,0000 z. for six days’ work.
And then the Caledonian crowd beat them badly : for
their first 12 months the mine produced ten tom of gold,
and distributed to its shareholders £572,000 in divs.

Priest-geologist Milne Curran declares that Aus-
tralian opals are invariably sold in London as Hungarian,

* * * *
Zeehanites (Tas.) are dead against Chinese, and
rose in revolt lately when a resident imported some to
grow vegetables for market. They held indignation
meetings and passed strong resolutions against John, and
also threatened to boycott their townsman. The Chinkies
left when they saw the storm raised, and subsequently a
motion was unanimously carried that their would-be in-
troducer and “ friend ” should leave with them. The
anti-Chinese feeling is as strong on the West Coast now as
in ’9l, when two luckless Celestials attempted a landing
and were thrown into the sea. Tas. miners remember the
East Coast and they are backed by others who had met
John in various parts of the globe. He is not wanted in
Zeehan—either as a “ cabbagee ” merchant or miner.
Which is unlucky for John as there are golden chances
for him in the West if he could but get his foot in.

The kind of men some mining managers are :

Quite English, you know !
A great, coarse, raw Geordie, wlio was having a
lot of stone crushed in a Gippsland battery, looked in to
collect, and was told that his gold had been seized under
a garnishee order. “ Garnishee! garnishee!” said Geordie.
“Aw dunno the blanlty blank; wha’s he?” A dapper
clerk was pointed out, and Geordie fell upon him, tooth-
and-nail. After beating his man, Geordie seized the
amalgam and made off, muttering : “ Garnishee, eh ! I'll
teech thee thoo [expression of endearment] to try an’
steal my gold.” The gentleman with the garnishee had
paid for the crushing before seizing. The law is now
hunting Geordie over a great maze of hills and creeks.

Ordinary sea-water contains half a grain to a
grain of gold to the ton, on which basis the ocean must
contain 75 thousand million tons of gold. What a chance
for a Westralian prospectus-monger !
* * * *
A new industry started in N.E. New Guinea is
sponge cultivation for the European market, a big lagoon
in the uninhabited Conflict group having been leased for
the purpose. The wild sponges already there are of good
type and readily saleable, but lessee Wickham is go inf in
for special cultivation with a view to greatly improving
the quality.

At a recent inquest there was found in a man’s
lung a half-sov. he had swallowed 25 years back. Only
with difficulty could a Westralian syndicate be prevented
from placing the corpse on the British market as a splen-
did investment.

The snap-shot fiend haunts St.
Hilda (Melb.) Esplanade on Sunday
afternoons. Rough for the married
tnan who happens to have 'acquaint-
ances among girls, or friends of girls,
that appear improper to his wary
bride. Whilst he is being casually
introduced to a chorus Tottie, in a
rather flagrant hat, the pocket-camera
operator takes tin; group and subse-
quently offers copies of the picture to
Ins pals at twopence a piece. They
may chance to travel round until
Arammta comes across one, where-
upon she buys or begs the material
lot ‘. a> first-class outbreak of domestic
strife. Sometimes the camera meanly
goes out of its way to lie, by substi-
tuting the head of a pious house-
holder for that of an abandoned
Johnnie taken in the aet of squeezing’
some female form divine. This trick
is presumably criminal, and there-
toro risky. Nevertheless, a St. Hilda
promenader has to reckon it in among
the awful things which may happen
to him on the Day of Rest.' Every
married man should instruct his wife
iii the dark ways of snap-shot photo-
graphy, so that when she se'es him
depicted in a truthful group, he may
lie able to persuade her that lie was
the victim of a wicked substitution.
The chances are that he will not
persuade her, but he may as well
give himself a show to try it on with
some outside hope of success.

Answers to co-respondents—Divorce de-

* * * *
Another Melbourne servant-girl has com-
mitted suicide all along of the fatal young man
who brings the milk. Romeo in this case’deposed
that he never quarrelled with his girl, but to a
rival milkman she imparted her determination tc
see Arthur no more, shortly after which the love-
lorn spinster swallowed a large dose of strychnine
-- in milk, probably. Strange, and yet not strange
this fascination exercised by milk-carriers upon
housemaids. It must be more or less attributable
to the early-morning visit, when their melodious
yodel arouses the girl from dreams of love and
brings her forth with the jug in a highly impres-
sionable mood. Beheld through the mists of early
morn and the vapor from heated fancies, Arthur
seems little less than a god. The fresh young
passion-flower is his, if he care to pluck it and
take it away in the five-gallon can.

A recent fashion-
able marriage—was it in
Adelaide ?- — recalls an
interesting yarn now
almost forgotten in-
teresting because so few
allegedly-white families
have aboriginal blood
in their veins. Back
in the “ fifties ” a
youth, the son of a city
notability, was sent to
gain experience in the
woolly west, and as he
was firmly affianced to
a very pretty girl, no
matrimonial dangers
were anticipated (Louis
Becke’s descriptions of
the seductiveness of
brown women weren’t
there to enlighten care-
ful parents in those
days). But, of course,
lie fell in love, head-
first, with a black-gin,
though, when the time
was up, he gaily re-
turned to town to wed
his white love. But,
like the copper-colored
bride in “ Turned Up ”
who was at “ Tilbury
docks, with a scent like
a blood-hound,” the de-
serted Venus (in ebony)
followed her faithless,
swain to the metropolis.
Then there, was a novel
tableau which ended in
a marriage after all-
only the bride was
black. And now that
years have softened
down the family com-’
plexion people see the,
advantages of a thing,
like that. Aboriginal
blood probably wouldn’t
always make the pale-
face more intellectual,
bud it would most cer-
tainly soften his wiry
manners. The Austra-
lian dark man has a
charmingly soft style,
as a rule.

From the reply to a question in Parlia-
ment it appears that, within 12 months, 668
couples have been wedded by the Sydney matri-
monial agencies, where marriages are not
‘‘solemnised” but only “performed.” At the
institution known as the “ Ministers’Study ” 94
marriages had been celebrated by the Rev. Alfred
.Tames Clarke, Baptist minister, and 110 by the
Rev. Lorenzo Howard, Independent Baptist
minister. • 1 1 ' ■ -• ■ .

port of their broken-down, parents in the Benovo
lent Asylums. In Denmark such refusal involves-
deprivation of all civil rights. Imagine the-

self unable to recover on the ground that he had!

mother.

At a recent fashion-
able amateur concert,
eleven friends and rela-
tives of one of the
singers brought bou-
quets and baskets of
flowers to present to
the warbler. She_ was;
by far the worst singer-
of the lot, and the rest ,
got no flowers. It was-.
a good illustration of
the silliness of the hor-
ticultural method of ap-
plause. Originally flow-
ers were thrown to a
singer out of sheer spon-
taneity and enthusiastic;
admiration ; now, the
flowers are brought a-
long in cold blood, and
the singer gets them all.
the same, whether she-
warbles well or ill.
The Monier arch, which supports a lot of
the trouble of the current N.S. W. Works Dept, en-
quiry, is essentially a grid or network of light iron,
embedded in compo. or concrete. Its ultimate
strength, as ascertained by recent experiments
m Europe, is greater than that of any compo-
site arch of similar dimensions, but its invention
is comparatively so modern that., no life can be
assigned to it with any degree of certainty. It
depends for its initial strength upon the mutual
cohesion of two foreign substances ; and for its life
upon the absence of chemical change in either or
both, whereby the cohesion between them is re-
duced or destroyed, or their strength impaired,
qjuery, whether a Monier arch erected and tested
now would, at the end of next decade, survive a
similar test?

Melb. Guvmentouse women follow Lady
Brassey’s lead in sporting violet as a dominant
color. The Brassey crowd walk to church ; no
carriage on Sundays. Six days shalt thou ride on
bikes or in carriages, but on the seventh thou shalt
give thy corns a little exercise. New command-
ment.
* * * *
The long arm of coincidence. A four-year-
old lad named Herschell was last week admitted
to Roma (Q.) hospital with a broken thigh, caused
by a tree falling on him. At the same hour,
on the same day, a year previously, the boy’s
father met his death from an exactly similar
accident.
* * * *
The new telephone woman shapes well.
The other day a business man rang up. “Yes,
yes, are you there ? ” said the female attendant.
“ Put me on to 306,” said the gentleman. “ Any-
thing else?” said the female voice. “Nothing
else,” said the business man. “Oh, yes, there’s
something else ! ” said the female voice. “ No,
there isn’t,” said the business man with asperity,
“except hurry up. Put me on to 306.” “Oh,
yes, there is something else,” replied the female
voice—“you should have said, ‘ 306, please !’ ”

The painted “ little peach,”
Who wend their way towards S.A.,
Must know before they start that they
Can’t pass until they bleach.

Recently, in Melbourne, a furiously-driving
milkman collided with a “ scorching ” bicyclist.
The cyclist was taken to the hospital; his machine
was carried to an adjacent machine-store. The
cyclist is the Terror of the Street—until he meets
with a milk-cart driver.
A number of Melb. parsons, interviewed
on the subject of Sunday cycling, profess to find
in the pastime evidence that the generation is
renouncing God and going back on Christianity.
The popularity of Sunday runs is proof that the
million are renouncing the common or garden par-
son and going back on his special brew of Chris-
tianity, which is the same thing—to the poor
parson. The parsons make God in their own
image, and contempt of their little plate and their
tiny opinions is contempt of God. The people
who cycle run away from the plate, and this is
forsaking Christianity. Only a wheel-tax, the
proceeds to go to the churches, would reconcile the
parsons to the bicycle—the wheel of ill-fortune.
* * * *
Does the air of Victoria particularly dis-
courage the development of leprosy, or is the
inspection carried out with less stringency than on
the Sydney side? Prior to last week’s deporta-
tion 18 Chow and 14 white lepers were inmates of
N.S.W. Little Bay lazarette, while at the Sorrento
(Vic.) quarantine-station only four Chinese slowly
decay. A large discrepancy.
♦ * * *
The 11. C. authorities are fencing in Shell
Beach, at Manly, which beautiful spot should, of
course, have been a public reserve, but was unfor-
tunately handed over to the church by the Robert-
son Government. The fencing-in will probably
lead to further investigation of the church’s title.
While digging post-holes for fences a number of
aboriginal skeletons have been discovered at the
beach ; also, some wondrous schnapper-skulls.
* * * *
Several Melbourne publicans, alarmed at
the provision in Peacock’s Factories Act giving a
half-holiday to barmaids, are about to discard
their fair attractions and substitute barmen.
The utter futility of Queensland Govt.’s
anti-tick legislation is shown by the fact that ticks
are now actually numerous oil the islands of the
Eastern coast. Presumed they were carried
thither by birds, blacks and dingoes.
* * * *
N.S.W. Justice Simpson in divorce reads
anonymous letters; Chief Justice Darley in
divorce declines to do so. Here is an interesting
question, not only in judicial etiquette but in law
procedure: Which of these methods should Judges
be encouraged to adopt ? Simpson says that in
one case a miscarriage Of justice was prevented
through his giving audience to an anonymous in-
formant. Has justice ever been defeated' by the
adoption of the other formula ?'

THE FIN-DE-CYCLE CHILD
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
At mother’s knee knelt little Dick,
His bed-time come, and all too quick;
Her hand caressed his flaxen hair
The while he lisped his nightly prayer.
“ God bless Mama, Papa and Ben,
And keep them safe, 0 Lord- i amen.’
Ilis praying done without mishap
Dick starts to climb his mother’s lap.
Mama reproved, “ You haven’t prayed
For Grandma’s safety.” All dismayed
Dick opened wide his eyes of blue,
“Shoo! What! Has Grandma got one too? ”

Abdul the Damned,” of Turkey, has had
a, narrow shave. Had the Armenians blown the
Imperial Ottoman Bank sky-high, nothing could
have saved him, for the fury of Bull and Crapaud
when smashed across the specie is a thing hideous
to hear of ; and the 1.0.8. is built on foundations
of English and French “ stuff.”
There are in Western Queensland 412
artesian bores with an average depth of 11851. feet
or an aggregate of miles. The total output is
210 million gallons daily. On the Saxby recently
a bore which had only occupied a fortnight in
sinking yielded two millions gallons daily. Ob-
servations on the Winton bore show that the pres-
sure of water is several pounds per sq. inch less
between midnight and sunrise than at any other
tune in the 24 hours. Artesian boring presents
quite a number of interesting scientific problems.
The sanitary arrangements in The Bulle-
tin new office at 214 George-st,, Sydney, were
supplied and fitted by the Sydney branch (335 Pitt-
st.) of the well-known London firm, Messrs. John
Tylor and Sons, Sanitary and Hydraulic Engineers.
This firm is the first of its kind in point of reputa-
tion, and the oldest as well, having been estab-
lished in the name of Tylor close on two centuries.
The London manufactory employs about 1300
workmen. The Australian branches charge prices
so low that the firm is placed in advance of all
competitors ; these prices are London prices with
only the cost of carriage added. And as the local
houses get large shipments at a time, carriage cost
is minimised, and the local customer can therefore
buy cheaper on the spot than he can by sending all
the way to London and waiting for the arrival of
his order. The local house, too, keeps right
abreast of the times, and has always the latest
designs on hand and the newest contrivances of its
London architects.*

Now that the summer months are coming
on the advantages of a Eureka gas-cooker, as com-
P®” with the common fireplace or stove or other
red-hot curse, become very apparent. It can be
lighted just one second before it is wanted and
turned out one second after the cooking is finished,
whereas the ordinary implement has to Jbe slowly
brought up to a red-heat and slowly cools down
again afterwards, and keeps the kitchen all the
time like Nebuchadnezzar’s furnace. The heat of
the gas-cooker can be regulated to a nicety,
whereas no one can .regulate the coal fire. It takes
just one second and one match to kindle instead of
. lm,| utes and a whole bushel of profanity. There
is no wood to chop or coal to be laboriously
flumped in, and the concern can be bought cheaply
on easy terms at the Gas Co.’s premises, Hay-
market, bydney, where instructions are thrown in
and lectures on cookery given free every Wednes-
day afternoon at 2.30.*

ATHEX.EUM Hali, Rev. Chas. Clarke.
I'he fact, that there is not, after all, very
much in “ Camille ’ was seriously borne in upon
the patrons of Sydney Lyceum on Saturday
night. Itis a story of how a woman and a man
gradually came to love each other in Acts I. and
1 1. ; how they were separated by the remon-
strances of his heavy father in Act 111., and how
the woman died, very much after the manner of
Little Eva, in Act V . There is information to
the effect that the woman had led a life of gilded
sin, and owed her jewels and her furniture and her
Hocks to the fact that her golden hair was
formerly suspended down her back, but this fact
is made as unobtrusive as possible. Barring the
dramatic riot in Act IV., when the good man and
the bad one meet in the lordly halls of sin, the
story is mainly the long, sad, dragged-out pro-
cession of one woman gradually dying of her own
reformation. It is a picturesque tragedy provided
that single pathetic figure is played by a consum-
mate actress, but, unfortunately, Mrs. Potter,
though she shows to much more advantage than
usual, is not consummate enough by a long way.
It is a romance of the soul—the pessimistic,
evening-dress soul with wormwood m it; and
either Mrs. Potter or The Bulletin, or both of
them, lack the right kind of soul. Also, there is
so' much waiting for the tragedy; the falling
heroine goes down so slowly with so very little in-
cideht in her fall, and hangs on to so many depres-
sing circumstances in her descent, and the
end is so long in coming, that “ Camille ”
grows wearisome. Dumas’ play is a fine word-
picture and a beautifully dressed woe that stops
continually to let the heroine reveal her soul, and
if she hasn’t enough soul to fill the stoppage
Mrs. Potter is less affected and less arti-
ficial than usual, but her utter want of elocutionary
ability and her want of many other things besides
arc against her. Still there is a fascination about
her Camille. The pieces of it arc mostly bad, but
the whole thing has a certain, or rather an uncer-
tain, beauty which flickers out here and there
quite unaccountably. Bellew’s lower is a good,
nicely-finished rendering of the part, but there is
nothing in it to justify Kyrle in coming such a
long distance to do it; if he had come about one-
third of the distance the proceeding might have
seemed a lot more reasonable. The Count de
■ arville, as done by Scot Inglis, is the strongest
and most notable piece of work in the play. These
are practically all the people who are in “ Camille ”;
a number of other people are partly in it, but in
the main it is a harrowing tale of two men and
one woman. ‘‘ School for Scandal ” will be on by
the time these high-toned remarks appear in print,
and that play is worth ten of ‘‘ Camille ” and a
little over,
I SK- *
Mr. Hodson’s co., at Her Majesty’s, is
faking a turn at “ True as Steel,” the drama with
tlie big steam-hammer in it, and that consistent
implement is working well and giving pleasure to
all. The drama is a kind of poor relation to the
“ Silver King ”; in each case the wanted indivi-
dual comes back after making a stupendous pile in
foreign parts, and disguises himself elaborately as
a deaf fossil around his old haunts in order to pick
up information that will tend to clear his charac-
ter,_ and at the critical moment both men tear off
their hair and whoop. But the present drama
does all these things in, a rather inartistic fashion,
and its chief dependence is on the big foundry
scene and the realistic steam-hammer which comes
down with a mighty wallop on nothing in particu-
lar. The. hammer’s apparent mission in life is to
descend on the head of Mr. Willard, the oppres-
sively good young man of the play, and' when that
person escapes his just fate there is no further
reason for its existence. Still, it may come down
the s P°t where it is most wanted before
' True as Steel ” departs to the place where it
ought to, have been all along, and there is still
hope. A lot of the popularity of the drama
depends on a young man who undresses himself on
the stage, and a lot more on a consumptive woman
who dies under harrowing circumstances in Act 1,,
and a very fat and ruinous comic drunk also helps
things along. The result of their labors is not
very definite, but they keep up a cheerful buzz of
incident while they last, and do a great deal of
irrelevant rushing at nowhere in particular, and
much hard drinking and amusing policeman keep
things moving.
* * * *
Charles Holloway has improved a little on
fiis last performance, and the curse which this
paper pronounced upon him is partly lifted, and
he is hereby released under the Hardened Offenders’
Act. Nobody else towers high enough above the
rest to present a specially good target for the brick
of deserved reproach. Miss Eugenie Duggan’s
ilewess is rather a pretty piece of work, and In-
man s villain is exactly like many other villains he
has done of late. The big mill-wheel is probably
the most novel performer in the co., and the
steam-hammer is a good second, and between these
fwo the drama will probably boom exceedingly
till further notice. If it had boomed much more
exceedingly than it did on the opening night, the
gallery would almost certainly have fallen in under
the burden of much excited meat, and there would
have been a copious inquest.

The long-deserted Criterion will boom
again on and after 12th September, when the
Brough Comedy Co. opens at its old stand. The
first production is “ A Mother of Three,” a farce
wmch has prospered immensely in London and
elsewhere ; and a number of other new pieces are
promised.

To-night (Thursday) and Saturday are the
last concerts of the Musin season at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall. The verdict of the city is,'as on
last occasion, that Ovide M. is the most
charming of fiddlers. His strong point is
not m the large, robust kind of performance; he
isn t a constant stream of dazzle and fireworks,
but there is grace and melody and refinement in
Ins performances, and he is a restful person in
some of his moods, and, also, he doesn’t look as if
he owned quite all the earth. Possibly hemay decide
to farewell a little more, or may be induced, with
great difficulty, to reappear for one more week -
they mostly are—but, at present, this is his very
last week -if lie doesn’t give way to extreme pres-
sure, or something.

«* * *
“A Trip to Chinatown” came to Mel-
bourne Princess’s on Saturday, and was received
with hysterical manifestations of delight. The
house, having made up its mind to laugh at every-
thing, laughed loudest at the things which, in
former times, might have passed away amidst a
solemn hush. The Bulletin didn’t join heartily
in the general glee when “ A Trip to Chinatown'”
was first produced in Sydney, and now that
another stamp of popular approval is imprinted
on the baby brow of “Mr. Chas. Hoyt’s amusing
musical farce-comedy,” this journal remains ob-
durate as ever.
* * * *
“A Trip to Chinatown” is a weak little
yarn with a number of poor little songs in it. The
chief musical item, judging from the programme,
deals with Harry Conor’s Experience in the
Bowery, and the tune of this well - sun"
and really funny dirty hasn’t been new
since the sisters Leamar sang “The Harem”
long years ago. A graceful contortionist,
Miss Bessie Clayton, appears to be the only dancer
worth mentioning in the company; anyway, Miss
Bessie’s one dance is much talked about during the
J.j hours previous to her doing the deed. Frank
Lawton, the gentleman who whistles wonderfully,
might as well get led up to in a like manner. His
whistling deserves quite as many preliminary puffs
as i the lady’s back-kicking, and seeing that the
whistle and the back-kick are the two uncommon
features of the entertainment, it is had policy to
puff one without the other. Another rash device
is the printing of plain “Patrice” upon the bill,
as though the small young lady in boy’s clothes
were specially important, What has Patrice done
that she should dispense with a prefix and divest
herself of a surname ?
* * * *
“A Trip to Chinatown” is run by 13
people, all told, and the majority of them don’t
tell mucli. Even chief humorist Conor fails to
explain the appearance of his name ip large type,
although lie very frequently conveys the idea
that he could knock considerable humor out of a
brighter character-part. There isn’t any snappy
singing talent among Hoyt’s comedians. Two
male members of the co., a pair of plump twins
seemingly, can warble a little in a strictly serious
strain, but when they wish to be funny they drop
into the ancient groove of mock Italian opera.
As a variety show, “A Trip to Chinatown”
will not stand much calm reflection. A num-
ber of points which memory would fain hang
on to. are not there, and with the finale
to. each act comes a strange sense of something
wanting m the background— viz. , a chorus. But
why argue agin a huge success ? The show
isn t a first-class joke, or a stylish piece of
frivolity, or a handsome spectacle, nevertheless it
must have the secret of success in its mechanism.
The stage business is carefully constructed, the
action rapid, and the thirteen characters work
together with an easy confidence that disarms sus-
picion. They will probably fill the Princess’s
until further notice.
** * *
Carl Hertz’s kinetoscopical views on the
sheet are about to leave the Opera House. The
pictures are probably inferior specimens, or Hertz
needs more practice in producing them, but their
faults don t count beside the great fact that Aus-
tralia has never seen anything so wonderful on any
previous sheet. Unhappily the kinetoscope inven-
tion is bound to be utilised for loyalty purposes
anon. The sheet of the future will display a faith-
ful representation of the bald-headed King Albert
Edward moving his lips in prayer for the welfare
of this distant land, whilst an assistant phono-
graph pours forth the words from a funnel.
** * *
“Romany Rye ” goes with increasing vigor
at _Melb. Royal, and the Nemesis which always
waits upon the wicked half-brother in the glossy
hat will possibly give him beans for yet another
week. Just here The Bulletin protests against
the infantile behavior of the half-brother at a
great moment. George Rignold has just rescued
his damp Gertie from the rolling deep, and is re-
ceiving homage from an excited fishing-village,
when the baffled owner of the bell-topper orders
a policeman to seize them on a charge of stealing
a pocket-book. It is such a ridiculously small
order under the circumstances that the manage-
ment would do well to cancel the line and let the
gentleman be removed in silence. Everybody
knows that the played-out intriguer has got to go
two minutes before the curtain falls, therefore
why not arrest him without comment when the
electric-bell rings ? “Romany Rye” is a strong-
minded melodrama except as regards the pocket-
book incident, and the realism of its marine
scenery made a visible impression on Gov. Brassey
when that nautical personage went to see.

Nat. Goodwin finished up his Melbourne
innings by pleading his ’Murkan accent as a reason
why the public liad been so unenthusiastic. It
wasn t clear whether Nat intended this for simple
truth or bitter satire, and, in any case, it was a
waste-of. speech. He and the co. in general were
most appreciated in pieces where their accent
served as local coloring and helped the show
along. The a very American adapta-
tion trom the T rench, is an improvement on the
utterly impossible English version. “In Miz-
zoura ” couldn’t be made Hinglish anyhow, yet it
was unanimously voted the best all-round effort of
the season.

The interest in the Brough Comedy Co. in
N. Queensland amounted to a craze. Beginning
the tour in Rockhampton, packed houses were
played to for the entire season of six nights ; to
meet the cries for more reserved places, it was
found necessary to abolish the pit and substitute
for it 400 chairs at 3s. each, the other prices of
admission being raised in proportion—2l6o seats
were booked for the six nights before the eo. even
reached the town ! The company’s success has
added a new province to the domains of the Aus-
tralian showman.
At Poverty Point.
News of circus-man Wirth’s death conies by private
letter from Japan. Just starting for China, he had a sun-
stroke, was carried back to his hotel, and died in two
days. Poor Mrs. Wirth received letters from her husband
asking her to prepare for his settling down in Australia.
With the same mail came a black-bordered missive which
she read last. It was from Wirth’s friends conveying
news of his death.
The Vic. Ministers turned up at Rickards’ show in Melb.
Opera House last week—Premier Turner for the first
time. The hard-working Peacock and Turner seemed
to enjoy the fun most; the stolid Attorney-General
Isaacs was apparently counting the shekels in the
house. Peacock’s famous laugh kept the audience in a
roar, sent a girl into hysterics, and at one time promised
to wreck the show. However, it might pay Rickards to
get the Chief Seo. there every night as claque. His yells
of delight at the mossy mother-in-law and wife’s-cold-feet-
in-bed jokes made all the married men look grim and
envious.

There is a melodramatic canon which ordains that only
the villain (and the comic man) shall say “ Damn ! ” The
hero never .says, anything worse than “ Confound!” When
he is very much annoyed he grits his teeth and exclaims,
m . But he is too good to swear, as a rule. There is
room for a play in which the hero (Baker, for preference)
should throw his soul into some good, manly blasphemy
when the villain, for instance, steals the title-deeds of the
estate, or accuses the hero of murdering his grandfather
Even the adventuress (vide a recent Sydney production!
can now say “Damn!” when she feels like it, but the poor
hero is left without any natural outlet for his feelings.

I sat in the front-row and watched her,
So I did.
By Jove, she was simply divine,
So she was.
I vowed that naught should deter me
From making - that fair creature mine ;
So I did 1
She danced, and laughed, and cavorted,
So she did,
Then skipped round the stage in a whirl,
So she did,
Till mj r heart was completely distorted
By love for that beautiful girl,
So it was !
I wrote and confessed of my passion,
So I did ;
Ay, confessed to that rising young star,
So I did ;
But her answer came back in this fashion :
“Rats ! I once went to school with your ma."
And she did !

Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest, buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal, direct tv
him. The firm has a reputation for straight dea>
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which n
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also u
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Personal Items.
The N.S.W. Ministry—Waist and Want.
Lord Brassey’s experimental farm in W.A.
is having another rest. Operations ceased a week
or two ago.
Westralian Chief Justice Onslow is a
grandson of Mr. Alex. Macleay, F.R.S., Speaker
of the first N.S.W. Leg. Council.
The Yic. Labor members speak rather
bitterly of Trenwith for neglecting to write to old
friends who put him in the way of tripping to
England.
A USEFUL DOG.
(A Little Bourke-street item.)
BILLY THE BOY: “ What yer call that dog, Spike—
a terrier? ”
SPIKE: “No, he’s a spotter.”
BILLY: “ A what-er? ”
SPIKE: “ A spotter. He keeps a sharp look-out on
my blind side, and when he spots a cop. he
gives me the office.”
The candidates for the Australian seat on
Privy Council B neh so far include one Anglican,
one Congregationalist, one Atheist, and one Spiri-
tualist.
Objections to C. A. Goodchap as N.S.W.
Protectionist leader : First, he’s a pensioner, and,
second, he writes worse verse than even Parkes’ or
Neild’s.
As a contrast to N.S.W.’s God-fearing
Ministry, the members of the present Victorian
Cabinet are said not to have a dogmatic belief
among them.
Melb. Age invariably spells Devil with a
small “d.” David Syme piously believes in “keep-
ing the devil down.” By the way, the Devil-
hats off !—always writes “david syme.”
Dr. Yackandandah Mueller, of snake-cure
fame, is shortly to be a party in an interesting
medical libel-case in Vic. County Court. Spooks,
cancers, medical journalese, rural etiquette, and
wild and whirling words are only part of the bill-
of-fare.
Thomas Ewing, M.L.A., chairman N.S.W.
Public Works Committee, occasional essayist,
fluent debater, and old Parkes’ “ young philoso-
pher, ” has turned serial-story-writer. He is also
an alleged poet of bulky proportions but strict
anonymity.
A pastoralist, well-known in the provinces,
left his widow <£40,000 a year or two ago.
She married a remarkably well-conducted young
fellow who had nothing ; but they unfortunately
managed to lose all. He has had to beg his way
back into his old billet.
The projected Australian rifle-corps, the
nucleus of which met in Sydney Town Hall last
week, looks, so far, more Protectionist than mili-
tary. ’ The last meeting was addressed by G. R.
Dibbs, C. A. Goodchap, F. A, Wright, A. Chap-
man, and R. A. Price —all in a row.
Premier Reid’s infantile love of sweets
wrecked a debate recently in N.S.W. Assembly.
The sugar question was on the board, and the
speaker was illustrating his remarks with a sample
of beet sugar in a bag. Reid promptly ate the
sample, and the orator broke down for want of it.
Reynolds’Newspaper says John Burns’
head is swelling. The Bulletin can name a
dozen people right off in the same case—five years
ago or less they knew they carried a very ordinary
potato atop ; now they feel it must be a pumpkin.
Just have a look at your own head. We mean
you.
J. F. Edgar, author of many clever Maon-
land stories in The Bulletin, leaves the sub-
editorial chair of the Melb. Herald and goes on
■to the staff of the Melb. Argus. Edgar is a
North of Ireland man who was planted young m
Maoriland and chummed much with the natives
thereof.
N.S'fW. Astronomer Russell has a rival up
Bowral way with a great local reputation. Some-
body interviewed him the other day, with a view to
ascertaining the secret of his success. It was this :
“There’s a (colonial adjective) old fool of a
weather prophet down in Sydney, and whenever
he says it’s going to be wet, I know it s going to
•be fine, and vice versa.”
The recently - deceased British Academy
President Millais is chiefly known to Australians
through two facts —he painted Pears’ soap-bubbles
picture, and he charged Simon Fraser, M.L.C. of
Victoria, 2000 guineas for painting his portrait.
And Fraser richly deserved it.
There is a J.P. recently appointed by the
God-fearing N.S.W. Ministry, sitting regularly
on the Bench near Sydney, who, less than a year
ago, was fined by the Bench which he now adorns
for “language.” The humor of it!
Dr. Neild, talking on the legs of his
imaginary Rosalinds, is not so thrilling as when,
at Melb. Shakespeare Society, he made the ladies
sit up and think of running away, while, apropos
of Falstaff, he described how his scalpel had curled
round a hundred dead drunkards’ hearts.
The Bulletin suggestion that bank-
manager Longmuir was not dead, but merely
lying dark, seems to have struck Melbourne
rather forcibly, and one or two cases of local
boomsters rumored to have been buried with-
out dying were freely quoted on ’Change and
in other financial circles. However, Long-
muir’s committal to the deep blue sea,- four
days before the P. and O. steamer Arcadia
arrived at Colombo in June, ’94, is officially
vouched for. This journal is not in a posi-
tion to prove an alibi for him. The defunct
scapegoat was, according to most accounts,
in an almost hopeless state of health when
he shipped from Melbourne.
W. B. Dailey’s children, it would
seem from the recent marriages of a son and
daughter, one in the Anglican and the other
in the Presbyterian Church, have not followed
the faith of their R.C. father, memorialised in
St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney. It is a singu-
lar coincidence that Dailey and Martin, two
of the ablest Australians, were led from the
faith of their fathers by their wives—a pair
of handsome and wealthy sisters. Dailey
after his wife’s death resumed _ active
relations with his church, but Chief-Jus-
tice Martin hardened as the years went
by, and repelled all clerical overtures on his
deathbed. Not, however, that any other
Christian faith had displaced their R.
Catholicism. Martin till the end and Dailey
for a time simply dropped their creed at the feet
of their wives.
» Demise : Chas. Dickens, jun. Australia
has two of his brothers, Edward Bulwer Lytton
and Alfred Tennyson —nasty names to fight the
battle of life on, conjoined with Dickens. Almost
as bad as Byron Moore. Dickens, jun., had all
his father’s industry, and managed to make nearly
as much money in an American tour.
Henry Clarke, M.P., the veteran member
of N.S.W. Parliament, has now been a representa-
tive for close on 30 years—having only been out of
one Parliament that is, last Parliament—since
first elected. He is 75 years, 6ft. 4in. high, as up-
right as a blue-gum, and personally most popular.
He bears no traces of age, his hair hardly being
grey yet. As Dr. Fiaschi says, “When they get
to that age, they’re the pick of the race.”
TICKS.
STOCK-INSPECTOR : “Has your mob been troubled much with parasites? ”
QUEENSLAND DROVER (thinking it a new name for boundary-riders) : “ We've not had much
trouble with per-p-p-parasites this trip ; it’s them stock-inspectors gives us rats."
W. T. Stead, in Review of Reviews
Mr. Fitchett, the editor of the Australasian Review of
Reviews, has begun the publication of a series of stirring
stories of British battles by land and sea in the Melbourne
Argus, entitled “ Deeds that won the Empire.” They
have caught on amazingly, and it is evident that young
Australia is waking up to the fact that it has been de-
frauded of its inheritance by the refusal of the authorities
to teach history in the public sshools.
Stead adds that this is a “ sin against the Holy
Ghost.” If history means the recital of revolting
stories of human abattoir exploits, splashing about
in pools of blood, and rolling on carpets of hacked
flesh and pounded bone, it would be well if it were
never taught anywhere.

sec., who goes to London as secretary of the local
Constitutional Club —a post secured for him by
Lord Kintore—is to marry a niece of N.S.W.
Attorney-General Want.

before, but let it go again.
During a vice-regal pro-
gress way-back in N.S.W.
Gov. Carington was intro-
duced to an aboriginal
monarch who had a mental
grievance. “ Look-a-here,
Missa Gubner,” began His
Majesty, “you boss alia
pos’-office, alia railway?”
The Governor admitted
that he did to a certain
limited extent. “ You boss
all policeman, too ? ” pro-
ceeded King Billy. “ Well,
then, you stopit Sargen
Murphy alia time kissit my
gin.” “ Oh, tut! tut,
Billy ! ” said his Excel-
lency. “ Oh, be dam tut!
tut! ” was the blackfellow’s
response ; “ suppose it Sar-
gen’ Murphy alia time
kissit Miss Gubner, you no
say tut! tut! then !
Poor Tarleton, the Sydney barrister, who
died not long ago, is said to have originally for-
mulated a libel “innuendo” now historical in
legal circles. There had been a row between two
neighbours, one of whom called the other “ a
(blanky) Wesleyan.” The drawing of the pleas
was entrusted to Tarleton, an intensely religious
man who, perhaps, didn’t like Wesleyans—he was
originally an Anglican clergyman and afterwards
Verted to R.C.’ism. “ A — Tr Wesleyan,”
the long, gaunt barrister: “meaning therew j
hypocritical person who used his religion as a cloak
for fraud.” „ ,

in active evidence.
W. C. Wentworth, a section of whose will
was the subject of inquiry in a Sydney court las*
week, spent the last ten years of his life in Eng-
land, where his intimacy with Archdeacon Dunbar,
his son-in-law, and present claimant of a sum of
.£25,000 from the “ Australian patriot’s ” estates,
was said to have made amends for the loss of old
Australian companionships.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Badly wanted in Australia—a Gaud-fearing
Ministry.
There is more surplice than surplus about
the N.S.W. God-fearing Ministry.
“We can’t stomach that,” said a S.A.
Tory M.L.C. recently. A new name. The
Stomach party.
The only Leg. Councillor who ever passed
from that body into S.A. Assembly is Copley,
leader of the Opposition.
Griffiths, M.P. (S.A.) says that province
is suffering from a negative and a positive evil.
The one is that the Heavens do not rain, the other
that Kingston does reign.
“ Women support the Divorce Bill because
there are not snffioient men to go round, and they
want to create a brisker circulation of males.”—
Kelly, M.H.R. (of Maoriland).
N.S.W. Labor-leader M‘Gowen : “The
present method of ascertaining the wish of the
people is barbarous, useless, and unreliable.”
Candor to recklessness. The “present method of
ascertaining the will of the people ” has ascertained
—M‘Gowen.
Out of 11,000 Q. civil-servants 5297 are in
the Railway Department. Nearly all the latter
are Labor-men, vigorous opponents of the Ministry
—for which merit the Ministry aforesaid has just
returned them the amount they were retrenched
some years ago. Moral ?
N.S.W. Government doesn’t feel quite
well over the Stuart (retrenched Civil-servant) v.
Gould case, right of action in which was conceded
to plaintiff by the Privy Council. Stuart, who
claims damages for allegedly illegal dismissal, is
said to have refused .£5OO to square matters.
The Argus talks largely of a big prospec-
tive Vic. produce trade with Glasgow, likely to
result from the visit to that hamlet of a glorified
middleman ca’d Sinclair, ye ken. The Argus
may whistle the tune of “ private enterprise ”
until she blows her teeth out ; any large trade of
the future will be strictly co-operative.
Mr. CASEY AT THE TEL! PHONE.
‘ Are you there? Is it the produce store? Well,
send me a hag of corn and a hag of chaff.
What’s that? Who’s it for, do you say! Why,
the horse, of course.” —(And then Mr. Casey
rings off.)
The logic of N.S.W. Foreign-trade Pre-
mier : 1. Let us kill local manufacture and crown
importation. 2. Let us hold an international
exhibition when local manufacture has been dead
three years. Then we shall know exactly how
dead it is.
Imports, like charity, should begin at
home. Raise all the stuff you can “on your own,”
before you import one grain, one rag, or one drop.
In the new Tas. Electoral Bill the absurd
wages qualification is retained and wqinen suffra-
gists are requested to wait a while. Attorney-
General Clark says he would be Democratic if he
could ; but, in Tasmania, the Upper House is the
whole team and the dog under the waggon ; so
Clark only asks for half a loaf in the hope of get-
ting a bit of burnt crust.
The recent want-of-confidence motion was
the seventh which the Ballance-Seddon (M.L.)
Govt, has survived in six years. It defeated the
first attack, in 1891, by a majority of eight, and
the seventh attack, in 1896, by a majority of 28.
No other Ministry in Australasia ever showed,
after six years of office, a majority three times as
large as the one it started with.
Hobart will return six members and
Launceston four under the Hare system of ballot-
ing tacked on to the new Tas. Electoral Bill. The
clause approving of the experiment of propor-
tional voting for these two cities was carried by
the Assembly without a division. The Upper
House is understood to have no serious objection
to the Hare system, so it is likely to become law.
Downie Stewart, an ancient Maoriland
politician, holds that if two people are married
for two years without having a child they should
be entitled to a divorce. Downie evidently
believes in the old theory that every woman’s
mission is to marry and have nine children, and
if she doesn’t begin fulfilling her mission after
two years, he thinks some other woman should
get the contract.
Premier Reid’s new financial policy is
about this : He says that every year’s revenue is
to be responsible for the year’s actual expenditure-
all of it, whether the expenditure is for that year’s
accounts, or the previous year’s, or any other
year’s. Then he charged about £900,000 of the
expenditure for the year to the accumulated defi-
ciency, and put it into the public debt, on the
ground that it was in payment of the previous
year’s accounts. And of such is the gospel of the
“ cash basis.”

extend the hours
of polling at Vic.
municipal elec-

working-men will

Major-Gene-
ral French, the
N. S. W. Com-
mandant, consid-
ers that the man
ufaeture of pow-

the hands of
Govt. “Thefirst

T. J. Byrnes, the “rising hope of the
Queensland Catholic party,” was the first represen-
tative man to give practical shape to the nebulous
desire of a certain set to have an Australian Judge
on the Bench of the Imperial Privy Council. He
incorporated the idea in the proceedings of the late
Federal Council, and claims the credit of having
thus inspired the ’Ome authorities to treat the sub-
ject as important and in a sense urgent.
The want of brains and of political fore-
sight of the “ Labor-party ” of Victoria was
amply shown the other day by its refusal to in-
clude “Employers’ Liability ” in its political pro-
gramme on the ground that Irvine’s Bill would
give all that is wanted. Why, even if carried (and
it probably will not be), it is a mere half-way
house to the reform needed before a man’s duty to
his own workmen is placed on the same footing as
his duty to other men’s workmen.
Victorian Leg. Council is postponing con-
sideration of all the “ objectionable clauses ” in
the Companies Bill. One of those postponed is
the clause forbidding any co. to set out in its
balance-sheet the existence of a reserve-fund un-
less such a fund actually exists. And this is one
of the provisions which the House of Directors
considers objectionable ! ! What a low opinion
of themselves these M’sL.C. must have when they
are afraid even to pledge themselves not to lie
about the reserve-fund !
Robert Stout’s constant tirade about the
Seddon Government’s seizure of the municipal sink-
ing funds would sound a lot better but for one
circumstance : It was Stout who passed the Act
authorising the seizure of the sinking funds. It
was also Stout who made such a learned muddle
of the said Act that instead of merely authorising
the annexation of the funds he was thinking of it
authorised the Government to run amok among
any others which might be. established in the
future, and to annex sinking funds in general. As
a rule, lawyers can’t make laws that will hold
water.
Sydney Evening News reproaches N.S.W.
Protectionists for not ceasing their useless,
foolish agitation, seeing that two successive elec-
tions have shown that the country is distinctly
against them. Curiously enough, the News
doesn’t urge the little band of Vic. Freetraders,
who have been shamefully licked at ten successive
elections, to give up their useless agitation and
accept the logic of things as they are, but whoops
them on to fight for the “ caws ’ to the bitter end.
The point of view again !
S.A. : The coloring matter imparted in the
English filter through which American cable-news
to Australia passes is shown by the items about
Bryan and the contest for the U.S. Presidency.
The cables said and inferred half-a-dozen nines
that the contest was a farce, silver-man Bryan
being nowhere. Yet Smalley, the Times man,
predicts a close run and an uncertain finish. In
the betting the most that has been got about
M‘Kinley is 2 to 1, whereas on Australian cabled
information it ought to have been at least 100 to 1.
The Maoriland Oppositionists are throwing
up the sponge. For weeks they have been buoying
themselves up with the hope that at a critical
moment some of the left-wing would join their
ranks. From this fond dream they have been
roughly awakened by the result of the want-ot-
confidence debate. Mitchelson has declared his
intention of retiring. Bell and Duthie are follow-
ing suit. The Nice People will now probably
affect the high-class American assumption of lofty
indifference to politics. Sour grapes. Ihe Oppo-
sition is now a fox without a tail; or rather, per-
haps, a fox that is all tail.
Frederick Sargood, in Vic. Legislative
Council, took exception to the proviso in the
Companies Bill that two directors as well as the
manager should sign the annual summary of the
co. ’s affairs. The grieved Sargood said that this
was dangerous, and added : “It is a matter which
concerns yourselves as directors.” By this remark
the foolish old draper frankly acknowledged that
the House wasn’t expected to legislate for the
good of the community, or in the cause of justice
or honesty or fair-dealing, but only in the selfish,
sordid interests of “ yourselves as directors.
How much latitude the Councillors want as
THE OLD MAN WAS BUSY.
KIND OLD LADY: “So your poor mother’s ill. And what is your father doing?”
CHORUS: “ ’E’s doin’ six months!”
directors ” is evidenced by the fact that they object
to be required to sign the accounts and thus make
themselves responsible for their accuracy. If a
director isn’t prepared to know enough about the
business to be aware whether the balance-sheet is
a lie or otherwise, or if he isn’t prepared to pre-
vent the issue of a balance-sheet when he knows
it to be a lie, then what is the use of paying him
to be a director? A director who isn’t responsible
for anything might as well be dead.
Sydney Daily Telegraph queries : If
N.S.W. Govt, is asked to “import, breed and
distribute, by sale or hire, well-bred cattle, chiefly
bulls,” for the improvement of dairy herds, why
shouldn’t it also be asked to lend (note that the
argument doesn’t even back up to the “ sale or
hire ”) “ best printing paper and new shiny type ”
(why not linotype ?) to newspaper proprietors ? A
more pertinent question is : As N.S.W. Govt,
carries by post (railway distances and hand-
delivery) newspapers free of all charge, why
shouldn’t it carry also dairy-produce free ? Why
does the Foreign-trade man in this protected glass-
house always heave this particular sort of brick ?
S.A. had a small surplus last year, partly
through charging sundry items of ordinary expendi-
ture to loans, and partly thanks to the Westralian
boom, which swelled the postal and telegraph
receipts immensely, and boomed the “fees ” item
through much registration of wild cats. If the
frenzy lasts there may be another small and
equally dubious surplus next year, though the
estimates show a deficiency of £7OOO ; and on the
strength of this possibility it has been decided to
throw away a good deal of revenue by raising the
income-tax exemption from £3i25 to Al5O. Queens-
land also had a small surplus last year, and is
taking care not to repeat the experience by abol-
ishing a large number of revenue duties. Each
province has a huge accumulated deficiency, but
neither Treasurer seems to have been struck by the
beautifully simple idea that it was blank foolish-
ness to sacrifice any revenue whatever until this is
paid off. Australasia never yet produced a Trea-
surer with grit enough to tell this country what a
fool’s paradise it lives in.
A FAMILY LIKENESS!
It now seems certain that G-i Mies will anti-
cipate dismissal by resigning the Vic. Agent-
Generalship at the close of the year. It is not
improbable, however, that Victoria will see a
general election before the year is out; if so, the
wily Duncan will be badly “left.” Successful as
Turner has been with his slow, plodding methods,
the re-appearance of so experienced a tactioian as
Gillies, with Wrixon, an old colleague, in the
Upper House, will mean a disturbing element to
the progress of Democratic legislation far more
serious than the spasmodic outbursts of the Mac-
Intyre-Madden type. Consequently,
garden Fat Men are watching the political baro-_
meter with undisguised trepidation.
The S.A. Ministry recently accepted invi-
tations to view the steamer Australian, which is to
trade between Adelaide and China. After the in-
evitable dinner the toasts of the Queen and the
Governor had been proposed, when Griffiths, M.P.,
mischievously instigated Prof. Watson to inter-
polate “the health of the Emperor of China,”
which was not on the programme. The chairman
frowned, but as several big Chinese merchants
were present, the sentiment had to be honored,
and the spectacle was witnessed of Kingston
and his colleagues drinking to a potentate whose
subjects, under a Bill now before Parliament, are
to be more rigidly excluded than ever from the
Northern Territory and from S.A. generally.
The effort made in N.S. Wales to create a
fruit-export trade by buying fruit with the public
money and sending it to England at the risk of the
community, is leading to bad language and smell-
ful accusations. This is how the allegations go:
N.S.W. has a God-fearing Ministry. Jacob Gar-
rard is a member thereof m an aggravated state of
piety. He represents a very depressed fruit-
growing district. The Government buys privately
thousands of cases of fruit at a good figure from
Garrard’s constituents, and thereby makes them
feel better. And sundry degraded unbelievers
suggest or infer that the whole business is mainly
a sop to Garrard's constituents, and Garrard is
understood to say that the people who say these
things are lost.
The erection of new and costly Parliament
houses in Sydney must, whenever federation
talk is on tap, raise the contentious question of the
choice of a Federal Capital. If the new Sydney
Parliament houses are to be the legislative halls of
a province only, then that province is about to
purchase a white elephant. On the other hand, if
the new buildings are conceived with an eye to
making Sydney the Federal Capital, it’s “Good-
bye, Federation.” Further, hasn’t Melbourne
already deeply regretted, and isn’t it now paying
through the nose for, the huge expenditure it in-’
curved on its over-gorgeous senatorial buildings ?
Keid’s action is merely the action of the small
municipality under a magnifying lens—the disease
that builds a palace when a cowshed would com-
fortably answer all purposes.
The returns so far show that M.S. Wales
has 16,321 and Victoria 21,177 people paying in-
come-tax on incomes of <£2oo a year and upwards.
If the returns of the five provinces—S.A., Tas-
mania, Victoria, N.S.W., and Maoriland —which
collect income-tax could be accurately compared—
which is difficult, owing to the different ways of
levying the tax —it would cast a lot of light on
the wealth of this allegedly great and rich country.
It might also incidentally burst up a lot of gaudy
fiction on the same subject. The statisticians’re-
ports go to show that Australasia is the richest
icountry on earth—and there is hardly one Treasurer
n it who isn’t suffering from insomnia and nervous
prostration and acute jumps in his efforts to keep
his province out of bankruptcy.

A Mother’s Appeal.
The following letter, dated 12th July, 1896, frond
Mrs. E. J. Grien, St. Hilda, Newton, N.S.W.,
appeals to all parents : “My little boy, now aged
14 years; suffered with dropsy, which extended all
over his body. He could not lie or walk about—
he was such a terrible size—even His Head was so
dropsical that it was twice its natural size. He
was quite blind, and His breathing wad very
labored. I took hind to a number of the cleverest
medical men of Sydney, but they cbuld do
nothing, and I began to think' that my pctor boy
was near to his last days. C)ne day when passing
a chemist’s window, I saw ‘ Warner’s Safe Cure
for Dropsy.’ I purchased one bottle, and when
finished the swelling seemed less, and his appetite
and digestion better. His digestion had been so
weak he could not take a drop of milk.
“ I was so delighted with the change, I sent for
more ‘ Safe ’ Cure, and now 1 can say this medicihc
saved my dear boy’s life, and trust that this may
be seen by other parents who have dear children
suffering. I would like them to see what Warner’s
‘ Safe ’ Cure has done for my son.”*
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclougli,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot of course be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Hull
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra*
clough.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Considerable credit for the success of the Aus-
tralian Eleven must be due to the management of Harry
Musgrove, and this is an opportune moment for Tub Bul-
letin to remark that his claims to undertake the job
satisfactorily were first announced and advocated in these
columns. Musgrove’s virtues as a conductor of travelling
show combinations are negative rather than positive. He
deserves credit for the absence of many difficulties that
haven t arisen this time, such as the Giffen difficulty and
the old-time grumbling re gate-money returns. The
evenness of the manager’s temper is reflected in the per-
formances of Trott’s men, who have seemingly remained
on good terms with themselves and one another from first
to last. Johns feels a bit sore, but the Victorian wicket-
keeper was the one superfluous member of the team as
things went, therefore his dissatisfaction doesn’t count.
* * * *
“Ranjitsinhji’s performance in the second test-
match :it Manchester” (writes “An Australian Abroad ”
to Tub Bulletin) “was unfortunate for England in that
it so thoroughly showed up the poor display of the other
members of the side. In the enthusiasm shown at the
close of the match, poor old ‘ W.G.’ was almost forgotten
annd the continued cheers for the Indian Prince.
“Ranjitsinhji was a Brahmin and is a prince of
j e Royal blood of some huge province or other in India.
Mt he preferred cricket to caste, and has been expelled
from his religion for breaking its most sacred rules by
living and eating with unbelievers. It is said that he
intends to go into Parliament, and, being a great favorite
with all classes, he will probably have a comfortable
borough found for him.
“ Iredale’s 108, Giffen’s 80, and Trott’s 53 in the
second test-match were all careful and g’ood performances,
but the team badly wants a hitter, if Graham gets going
he is by far the best of the lot in this respect, but he
rarely does get going, and many of the matches have
proved most tedious to watch.
The ninth Australian Eleven has, so far,
shown that, with a strong and determined executive com-
mittee and a firm manager, there is little difficulty in
avoiding dissensions. Moreover, the general conduct of
the team has been excellent. It is not expected that 15
cricketers are going to behave like 15 saints doing penance
and they don t ; but, so far, the present team has
managed to greatly improve the reputation previously
attached to Australian Elevens.
' Richardson bowled better than ever before in
the second innings of the Australians at Manchester test-
match. lor just over three hours he bowled without a
rest, and Ins last over was as good as his first. The speed
never slackened, and for a fast bowler it was wonderful
.f variety of balls he sent down. He uses a clear, brainy
intelligence m his work, and he can break both ways with
ins swiftest deliveries. Jones was suffering from a strained
side m the match and couldn’t do much, but he is never
likely to rival the Surrey man, and will never earn the
reputation that Spofforth, Turner, or Ferris did.”
Ernest Jones’ Adelaide friends cannot under-
stand ..he cable news that the Sussex engagement has
v-u trough, but recent English papers mention facts
which suggest the solution. Jonah’s salary of £365 a year
was to be raised by subscription, one gentleman promising
is «\year. Enthusiasm like this frequently wanes, and
probably Jones recent ill-success has been the cause.
Jonah is probably sorry now that he didn’t accept the S. A.
offer of £2OO a year for five years.
