A, Toowoomba Australian explains how the
thud test-match was lost., “ What really puts the Aus-
tralian team off,” he says, “is having to play against a
black potentate. Black princes in this country always
wear brass plates, and a prince without a plate is a source
of great embarrassment to the Australians. Added to the
worry attaching to the proper pronunciation of the plate-
Jess Ranjitsinhji’s name, there is always the hauntino-
thought : ‘ Me gib it plug ’bacca it go out first ball ! ’ And
then it doesn’t.”

The rainy weather put the Australians at a dis-
advantage on two occasions when Trott lost the toss -
with Surrey and Somersetshire—but it also saved them
“' J"’? probable defeats. Had every match been played
out, their proportion of wins to losses would have been
much reduced. On the other hand, they can say that no
English team has made 400 runs in an innings against
them, although the scoring in inter-county matches this
season has been exceptionally high.

* * » *
Australian bowlers, being- a large assortment,
are not required to work nearly as hard as tile best
English pros., who, as a rule, have to keen pegging away
steadily with very little assistance from their inferior
colleagues. For instance, in any given number of weeks
this season, J. T. Hearne, Richardson, Attewefl, Briggs
and Mold, collectively, bowled almost twice as many overs
as Jones, Trumble, Trott, Giffen and MTvibbin.' The
county cracks never stand out- of a match unless they
happen to be crippled. The fiction that the Australians
get overworked will not bear the test of figures, for
llearne and Richardson took 298 wickets between them in
about the same time, or perhaps a fortnight longer than
the time, in which Jones and Trumble captured 101.

* * * *
Probably the best-known portrait of Bert Harris,
the English cyclist in Australia last season, shows the
speedy youth on a machine fitted with a wondrous frame-
work of bent iron, one arm of which half encircles his
waist, what this extraordinary affair was half the
cyclists in Melb. puzzled their brains to conjecture, yet
not one hit the mark. The idea emanated from a man
wno, horrified at the stooping position of most wheelmen,
devised an apparatus whereby the weary scorcher would
be held in a partly upright position, his diaphragm resting
< i a a curved and padded bar. The inventor paid Harris
L2O to have his photo, taken with this machine, only
stipulating that the Leicester flyer should stand before no
other camera for a period of 12 months.

Davison, the Hillgrove bikeman, is a miner,
winch looks well for his future championship chances
Aquatic and pugilistic history shows that the world’s
champion, in sports requiring strength and staying
power, invariably is manufactured out of the manual
laborer.

Anti-gambling petitions have rained continu-
ously into Las. Parliament during the session, and the
Gaming Suppression Bill has just made its appearance.
Every known form of gambling is squashed flat, but the
last clause gives power to the Gov. in Council to 'render
inoperative all the penalties of the Bill re lotteries and
totalisators. \yjiereas the anti-gamblers howl for the
demolition of Tattersall, and beseech Sir Post-office Fysh
to stop all correspondence after the manner of the other
provinces. Sir P.O. thinks of the £13,000 revenue Tatter-
sal! brings him, and is exceeding sorrowful.
In pleading for plaintiff in the CLIPPER-Gilmore
libel-case, at Launceston, the other day, barrister Purves
scornfully referred to “ this class of newspapers which everi
insinuate that owners of racehorses ihstruct trainers and
jockeys not to win a race.” This ,is a sore point with
Purves (in his legal capacity), and one that Champion
probed cruelly.
* * * *
, Carbine’s son, Charge, ran a really big race in the
Hampden Stakes. Last in the straight, he came through
his horses in great style, and Hopscotch just struggled
home in front of hini with a neck to the good. Coil
finished third, three lengths off, humping a 101 b. penalty.
Charge, as a maiden, had a 31b. allowance, but he finished
so full of running that it is hard to take any line from the
colts’ respective running as to their Derby chances over
the extra three furlongs.
* * sjc *
Flying Hopscotch set a cruel pace in w.f.a.
Hampden Stakes, and had most of bis 14 followers upset
at halfway. Long’s chestnut champion was the king of
the crowd, but Charge finished fast alongside, and would
have won a few yards further. Penalised Coil, third,
hadn’t the “go” of Charge, and, despite the difference in
weights the Carbine colt may prove the better on Derby
day.
People who love a first-class horse think that his
heavy weight won’t stop Hopscotch in Epsom mile. He
won with 131 b. less last year in very hurried time,
after running unplaced in Hampden Stakes a fortnight
previously. This latter race he won last Saturday. Dal-
meny, third in the Epsom last year, has only 7.9 this, and
should prove a good investment.
* * * *
The Cup star, Reka, galloped queerly in Tatt’s
Spring Handicap last Saturday, but would have been
much better than third had he not swerved at the Leger.
The finish was burnished gold for the books, 14 to 1
Checkmate landing from Leonidas, who is due for a race
at the A.J.C. Reka was 5t02 in a 25-field, and his
owner never dreamt of defeat.
Pivot’s unlooked-for all-the-way win in Sandown
Park Handicap, last week, has put him in credit for Melb.
as well as C. Cup. The Svviveller horse is quite fast
enough to support the prediction of an authority, quoted
by Tiib B-ulletix, that a hurdler would win at Fleraington
this year.
* * * *
Gozoczar’s late efforts don’t foreshadow the
classic prospects he possessed last year. Sabretache and
Fucile_ aren’t likelys, but Charge is a hot possibility.
Anything that can give Hopscotch a gruelling at w.f.a. is
no_mouse. Tirb didn’t saddle Saturday, but if he, Reka,
Coil and Co. all start in Rand wick Derby there should be
an impressive scene and much change of currency.

Newman pulled out for the big money at Rand-
wick on Saturday immediately after Hampden Stakes;
made play most of the way, and finished fourth, beating

between.
* * * *
Prince Harold went near to beating the Aus-
tralian two-mile trotting record, 4min. 57sec., at Brighton,
Sydney. He covered the first half-mile in 1.13, second
1.14 6-10, third 1.14 6-10, and last in 1.15 3-10 ; in all, half
second behind Honor’s record. As a rule, the Prince is a
good breaker, coming into his stride straightaway, but in
the last round he made a bad break, which lost him the
record.
* * * *
Frank Agan is the pacer that was, at latest,
spoken of in America as likely to cross over to take down
Buckland s Fritz in N.S.W. He lately paced a mile-heat
in 2min. 6Jsec. Should a horse be sent over, the national
character of the event would warrant the A.J.C. in
taking the matter in hand, and running the heats off at
Randwick. Property worked up, for a Saturday after-
noon, a record attendance could be counted on.
* * * *
The omissions from the A.J.C. jockey-list came
as a staggerer. Apropos , anyone doing “ a hit of funny
business ” had better try it on with a horse owned and
heavily backed by a powerful racing magnate. All the
trainers* however, can go on for another year. The dif-
ference between a jock, and a trainer often is that one
rides to order, and the other orders to ride.
* * * *
A clerk and a bit of pencil are to be bookie’s
sole appurtenances in future. Nondescripts who race
round agitatedly, and. signal betting and whatnot, have
been vetoed b,v the A. J.C. as objectionable and unneces-
sary. So that any individual who now tries to earn an
honest shilling making hand-signs, taking off his hat,
hitting his chest and frantically waving his ’kerchief is
liable to be run in as a lunatic.

ful living chance.

The late George Davis, 3V1.L.C., was not quite

hummerlea in ignorance of their real grit, and he held

in good company. Tim’s sensational victory in the Caul-
field Cup was, if not a fluke exactly, a thing'that couldn’t
have happened under ordinary circumstances for the same
event. Yet Davis believed the horse to be a real good
one, and backed him for two subsequent Melbourne Cups.
A rural crowd of books was recently milked in
a most cold-blooded way. A hurdler, pulling up appar-
ently lame, his owner interviewed the chief “ dead meat ”
merchant. After inspecting the corpse, bookie.success-
fully offered a consideration not to scratch. Well-backed
on the day the prad made one of five starters. One fell
and the “dead ’un” ran off. The other three baulked
persistently, however, until he puiled round and won
It was a peculiar rase,” remarked the local horse-re-
porter, but the victory was a popular one.”

K. : The only way to stop roping—when the
Stewards smell anything nasty, let them take the stiff’un
off at the last moment, and put up a jock retained by the
club.” But how would this suit the books of betting
stewards?
* * * *
’Spite all efforts, racing languishes at Broken
Hill, and almost every meeting results in a loss. But a
proposal to form a course just over the S.A. border at
Cockburn, 34 miles from the Hill, is taken up enthusiasti-
cally—and the meetings will boom. All because the tote
can be used there. Yet they say that the machine cruels
racing ? Meanwhile, the efforts to keep the sport going at
Broken Hill itself are practically abandoned.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey develops turfy tendencies in a
mild degree. He talks of his forthcoming trip to Sydney
in September as “ going over for the races.”
* + * *
The breeding of Saddle-Up, by Mana-Ddloii
inare, is not attractive, and the same negative objection
applies to Excel, another Melbourne horse that has lately
been “ starring ” among less-seasoned performers. Any-
way, it is early to think of these winter blossoms for the
big spring handicaps.
* * * *
The Australian starting-machine makes rapid
American headway.
* * * *
The “ confidential man ” of a very prominent
Australian racing-stable is shadowed like a suspected
person when an event is approaching. Until the owners
have got their money on he is kept an absolute prisoner
and is not allowed to stir beyond defined limits. All his
outward correspondence is also carefully examined.
* * * *
Babel’s 13st. 71b. in a flat race at Longreach (Q.)
is a record. And 28 were nominated. Longreach, by the
way, is the only place in Australia where the flat minimum
is 7.7 —a stone higher than usual.
* * * *
Defunct Nordenfeldt’s place in Mr. S. Hordern’s
stud is to be filled by the St. Simon horse, Hautbrion, a
fair performer ; but he never justified his 5000 gs. valua-
tion as a yearling.
* * * •
Estimated that 320 racers and 220 ponies are
now in training in Sydney metropolis. A costly crowd for
the public to keep in oats.
* * * *
In an easily-picked galloway race lately the
favorite’s rider suddenly said—“ Hang it! My whip’s
dropped.” “ Here ! take mine,” eagerly yelled three
spider pilots simultaneously as they rounded the turn for
home. “ Always do a cove a good turn when you can.”
* * * *
The system under which Sydney papers publish
starting prices appears to require revision. Some books
quote different figures, and the papers are seldom
unanimous. Of course, stay-at-home s.p. backers are
always paid on the shortest quotation in the case of
winners.
* * * *
“If you only have one horse and want to make a
coup you must ‘wait,’” said a racing-man the other day,
when talking turf reform. “I had a prad once, trained
him myself, and waited six months. Of course, I was
following another business. Backed from tens downwards,
he landed me the pool of my life. My house, land, and
section adjoining, came from the proceeds. Yes, he
wasn’t a bad horse. It was the only race he ever tried
for. That’s him there in the buggy.'
• * • •
In 13 out of 18 Caulfield Cups the winners have
■carried less than Bst. It is time another Westminster,
Torn Kirk, Culma, or Paris came along.
*** b *
The steady tug-of-war between the V.R.C. and
the Flemington and Essendon people, with regard to the
closure of Flemington racecourse for pedestrians, yet
continues. The local bodies have just emphatically put
their feet down, and there is a deadlock. It has often
■been pointed out how Byron Moore sneaks up to charging
for admission on race-days.
* * * *
S.A. Advertiser, the other day, contained a
horse-for-sale advt., ending thus, “ Reason selling, no
■use.”
* * * *
A Sydney jock, put off by many promises,
stalked into a pony-owner’s house, the other day, with a
limb of a tree and demanded settlement of an old debt.
He was unsuccessful, but wheedled out of the terror-
stricken sport an old saddle, 55., meerschaum pipe and a
ticket in Tatt.’s sweep. Opening the alleged ticket later
■he found it was a written cure for corns.
* * * *
A Maori with limited ideas about horse-produc-
tion sent his old draught to a blood stallion to secure a
“ racer.” Some time afterwards he wrote the owner of
the sire : “That foie him no tam good. He killed by
lightnin’. Me send horse again. Me no want grey foie
thish time. Kapai bay allee same Carbine.”
* * * *
A Sydney pawnbroker tells of a woman who
tearfully implored an advance of 2s. 6d. on an old brooch.
He cut her down to Is. 3d., though she went on her knees
and said the half-crown would keep her children from
starving for a week. “ Man’s inhumanity to man,” &c.
Two hours afterwards he saw her betting in thripennys
■outside a pony course gate, where colleetion-plate layers
■do biz. on race-days.
• • • •
A leading cross-country jockey is reported to be
■slowly dying from the effects of croton oil, taken to reduce
weight.
* * * *
A Victorian horse that has not yet appeared in
public is greatly fancied for the next V.R.C. Derby by his
shrewd “ stable.” All things considered, punters should
wait another couple of months before rushing in to back
Newhaven and Coil.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —One unlucky drawer of ahorse
in Tatt.’s sweep on the Spring Handicap wired the gilded
■owner £lOOO to 0. He replied demanding one-third of the
prize for first, second, or third place.' This profitable
branch of the racehorse industry badly needs lopping.
To do this Tattersall Adams should swear off telegrams
and let his clients discover their luck through the ordin-
ary “ result slip.” Under the present system, horse-
owners are aware that drawers of their horses have been
'notified,- and calmly await the usual wire offering a third
•of the prize, failing which, they “don’t intend to start.”—
.Blackmail.
* * * *
Mr. C. M. Lloyd thought it was despising for-
'tune’s favors to keep Newhaven at home, and the chest-
nut idol crosses over through his intervention. Distant
fields are green, but the Randwick fields this month won’t
race as green as they look.
* * * *
Hordern’s ambition to follow in the winning
■steps of the late James White has been unrealised so far,
■despite a big string. The stable is unrepresented in
A.J.C. Derby, its half-a-dozen entrants having proved
below standard.
* * * »
The M.L. trio now located at Randwick Coach
and Horses should infuse some life into forthcoming stick
events. Mentor Hope rode the M.L. G.N. Steeple winner
the other day. Hope and Clark have secured this coveted
event thrice each in six successive years.
Toreador’s daily gallops don’t excite much en-
thusiasm. The Skipper appears to be more forward, and
may be the stable’s special weakness in the Metrop.
* * * *
The death of the noted three-quarter, Madigan,
makes the sixth decease in the Maori-England team.
Nearly all from consumption.
* * *
The United Australasian Axemen’s Association
bills its sixth annual contest of chopping and sawing, at
Latrobe (Tas.), for Novi 9. Prizesi £4OO. >
* ♦ * *
From an old Sydney file (Dec. 8, 1845): —“At
the ‘ Woolpack,’ Petersham, a wager was made that a man
would go one mile without touching the ground, and
would not be drawn or ride. It was accomplished on a
bicycle.” The “Woolpack” was on Parramatta Road,
Petersham, next to the old stock-yards, recently demol-
ished. The old hotel stands to-day in the form of a
rambling private cottage, and the old wooden water-
trough is stowed away in the backyard.
* * * *
Rupert Guinness, son and heir of the Dublin
stout brewer, won the amateur sculling championship at
Henley Regatta. Rupert is said to be a splendidly-built
fellow; but not clevah.
* * * •

* * * *
Brisbane Commercial Rowing Club has had very
watery luck. In ’9O, a good flood swept away their shed.
Three years later, another shattered £5OO worth. Then
the hulk Beatrice was secured, and converted into a sort
of Noah's Ark. She went to the bottom a fortnight ago.
The cheerful members are now raising funds for a build--
ing which all the floods in a decade won’t shift.
* * • •
At a rabbit-coursing match at Windsor (N.8.W.)
a hot favorite was put out in the final by a scrubby out-
sider. Then a protest was lodged against the winner
because he had a long tail. And the committee upheld.
* * * *
The “false rail ” at Caulfield, intended to pre-
vent crowding on the inside of the turn into the straight,
has exceeded its purpose by causing the horses to"get
hunched on the outside, and the V.A.T.C. committee has
taken the safeguard down for fear of accidents. A milder
form of Mat. O’Shanassy’s remedy will probably be intro-
duced after further consideration. The idea of widening
the curve, so to speak, without widening the course seems
rather Irish ; also the wisdom of fixing a false rail, instead
of shifting the original inside rail, is not quite clear. But,
seeing that something ought to be done at Caulfield, the
recent experiment deserves an effort to improve upon it.£
* * * *
W. R. Wilson, The Bulletin fancies, will have
something better than Nada for the Melbourne Cup. All
the same, the mare is likely to win a race at Randwick.
* * * *
Six-year-old Malula, Marusa—Maluma, Mal-
volio’s brother, winner of Mentone (Vic.) Handicap,
should have a big say in Caulfield Cup with only 7st.
* * * *
M.L. Govt, is further curtailing tote permits,
the big metropolitans being granted four instead of five.
Compared with overdone Sydney, even five meetings a
year seems a mere grain of salt in a bag.
* * * *
In 1889, vet. surgeon Dunlop, of Belfast (Ire-
land), meeting his son on a boneshaker and fearing for his
spine, wrapped a piece of garden hose round the wheel
and fastened the end with melted rubber and glue. Next
week he smelt a fortune, and the Dunlop tyre was
evolved.
* * * *
The other day in Charters Towers a horse bolted
with a dog-cart. Two of the occupants were thrown out,
the third, a boy, being left alone in the trap. A woman,
seeing the danger of the child, rushed into the street,
seized a billet of wood, dealt the horse a blow that laid
him out insensible on the roadway, saved the child, and
walked away quietly unconscious of having done anything
remarkable. Was it not said of Abimelech— ■“ A woman
slew him ” ?
* * * *
A month is the maximum period for which stiff
ponies are “ rubbed out” on the Melb. courses. Were it
made any longer they couldn’t breed ponies enough to
keep the sport going.
* * * *
Dan O’Brien got rid of his ghostly spirit of bad
luck Saturday, when Patriot, Cronnvell up, smothered a
long-drawn-out juvenile field. The racy-looking young-
ster is another of Autonomy’s, and is a likely Mari-
byrnong Plater.

The nomination of the American Demo-
cratic candidate for the Presidency seems to have
been based chiefly on. wind. This sort of bi-metal
talk : “ You shall not set on the brow of labor a
crown of thorns ; you shall not crucify mankind
on a cross of gold.”
‘ ‘ I can’t help stealing, your worship ; I
have to take everything I see that I fancy,” said a
Sydney woman whose vogue appeared to be to
“ take lodgings ” and to walk off with portable
valuables. But kleptomania is only a recognised
luxury for the rich and influential.
Sandwich men are out-bye-lawed in Melb.
One was fined the other day for appearing in
Bourke-st., as one item in the rivalries of three
local bootmakers calling themselves Sir William
Olive, Sir Thomas Olive, and Lord Clinton.
These humorists presumably mean by their as-
sumption of rank that all snobs should be en-
ilobbled.
, Ed. Bulletin, — lie black-cockatoo’s egg—-
Van Demon’s “roc’s egg amongst collectors.”
The black cockatoo “nests” in March; all other
N.S.W. birds from Aug. to Nov. Few collectors
know this, but to those that do the b.c.’s egg is
not the “ roc’s egg.” I have taken several nests,
each one in March. They nest in the hollow of a
tree, and only lay one egg. During 18 years’ col-
lecting, my “ roc’s egg ” has been that of the jack-
snipe (not the oft-mistaken painted-snipe). Can
any of your readers, &c. ’—Leslie M. Oakes,
Parramatta.

Dear Bulletin, —Your par. referring to
G-. W. Rusden recalls a Brisbane incident of some
years ago, and illustrates to perfection how his-
tory is made. At the time Gfresley Lukin, that
good friend of writers, ran the Couriek and
Queenslander, and the late Carl Feilberg was
the leader-writer. Lukin, actuated by humanity
and the fact that there was a dearth of local
topics, thought it a good opportunity to bring for-
ward the treatment of the‘blacks 'in the outside
country. The subject “took”; Feilberg wrote
the leaders and I answered them under "a pen-
name in_ lengthy letters-, posing as “the blood-
thirsty pioneer.” Feilberg and I used to discuss
the matter beforehand, as to what I should write,
on which he could found a good leader in reply.
Considering that I have always Written (under my
own name) advocating the forming of reserves for
the natives, and the exclusion 'of all Whites front
within the reserve boundaries, as the ohly ineahs
of prolonging the existence bf the race,' I Was
extremely amused when I read Rusden’s history
and found the whole Queenslander correspond-
ence gravely and seriously quoted with tlie follow-
ing remarks appended : —
Who shall say that .Swift's description of his couhtrv-
inen, as a pernicious race of ddiohs v'erinin Suffered id
brawl upon the surface of the earth, Would have heeii
hyperbolic if th’ey Were as Worthy of it as the Queens-
correspondent. In A foreign land his letter might
eVeh seen! a veiled satire upon the system professc'diy
‘extolled. But iii Queensland the sincerity of the writer
Was knowh and he Was hot without admirers. To such
base uses may the product of modern civilisation be re-
duced When exempt from the chastening influence of reli-
gion and of a Well-ordered society.
Alas ! I was the product of modern civilisation.—
Delcomyn.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

Vic. Comity Court Judge Casey has an-
nounced from the bench that it is much more
venial to steal £lO from Baronet Clarke or
squatter Pursey Chirnside than to steal the same
from laborer John Jones.
The free population of New Caledonia now
numbers 13,038; the convicts, 10,757; and the
natives, 27,238. The oldest inhabitant is the Pole,
Berezovski, who tried unsuccessfully to assassinate
Czar Alexander 11. in Paris, 6th June, 1867.
According to official returns, it costs £4O a
year to feed the horse of the Vic. Chief Commis-
sioner of Police, and £5O to feed the horse of Col.
Tom Price; ditto for the horse of Col. Hoad. It
costs, however, only £24 to feed the horse of a
collector of Customs, whilst the horse of another
Customs officer has to live on £l2 a year—Stock
Inspectors’ horses have to live on 10s. a week. A
Colonel’s horse apparently eats twice as much as a
Stock Inspector’s, and four times as much as a
Customs officer’s.
The excellent Lormer, J.P. (who was
presented with a prize for virtue in the shape of a
cheque for £133 the other day) says that Justice
(m Melbourne!) is a female sitting down with her
eyes uncovered—possibly to see which was the
rich and which the poor applicant for justice,
whilst the scales are almost hidden from view in
her lap. He was referring to Justice as repre-
sented on the facade of the law-courts: —
Ah, Justice in these Southern lands
Appears in different guise :
The scales are no more in her hands—
The scales are on her eyes.
Obit : ‘ ‘ On Saturday, Aug. 16, at Laven
der Bay, Joseph Wyatt, formerly of Wyatt Bros.,
°f, Pitt-street, Sydney, eldest son of the late
William and Mary Wyatt, who emigrated to
Sydney in 1828; aged 70 years.” This “free”
emigrant came out with his family in a specially-
chartered ship laden with his stock-in-trade, and
started as an ironmonger in 1829 on the site of
David Jones and Co.’s warehouse inGeorge-street,
Sydney.. He then shifted to the bank of' the Tank
Stream in Pitt-’street, and in 1850 removed to the
site of the Royal Arcade, near the School of Arts.
In 1837 he began to build the Victoria Theatre in
the same street, now the site of Harris and Ack-
man’s warehouse. The theatre was opened 26th
March, 1838, with ‘‘OthelloArabin(fromHobart
Town) as the Moor, Spencer as lagq, Mrs. Win-
stanley as Desdemona. The Vic. suffered the
usual fate of playhouses, and was burned down
23rd July, 1880.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic:!, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
.main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
Robert Stout, Maoriland’s wonderful ex-
Demoorat, declares himself against the Old Age
Pension Bill and the State Fire Insurance scheme
—the latter because “it is altogether in aid of the
propertied classes and an unwarrantable interfer-
ence with. private enterprise.” Here Stout con-
tradicts himself badly ; the propertied classes own
the private insurance offices, and the scheme can’t
be in aid of them and an unwarrantable interfer-
ence with them at the same time. It is a fashion
nowadays for Fat Man to be more democratic
than the Democrats, and when he opposes pro-
gressi ve legislation he generally does it on the pre-
tence that it is, in some impossible way, legislation
in favor of the propertied classes, and that he is
against the propertied classes, though he doesn’t
look like it, and wants to put them down. Stout’s
wild lament that for the State to annex the profits
of the fire insurance biz. will really, in some
obscure way, subsidise the capitalists is a very thin
story.

0 MINING COMPANIES, BATTERY OWNERS
AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Tregurtha’s
Patent Battery Plates.
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with. The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs”. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold and pyrites,
allowing the sand and water to flow away. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtained by these plates
than by the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, where
the gold is associated with some foreign mineral, which
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in con-
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from
J. MILLER <sc CO.,

TheMaoriland lady-help has been exempted
from the Bill providing a compulsory weekly half-
holiday for the domestic dish-smasher, therefore
there is going to be a big increase in the demand
for lady-helps very shortly. The plain, garden
Bridget is to get her half-holiday, but the lady-
help is left out in deference to her feelings or what
the House thinks are her feelings, in order to
differentiate her from the Bridget. The lady-help
already gets about half the wages of the ordinary
slavey, and does about twice the work, and occu-
pies an anomalous position as a sort of poor rela-
tion or orphan that was found on a doorstep ; and
this further distinction should about break her
heart.
Gist of a Maori petition to the M.L.
pakeha Government: Masterton and Porirua
Maoris consented to the Crown granting certain
lands to the pakeha Bishop (C. of E.) of Maori -
land, on the distinct promise that schools, &c., for
Maoris should be erected and maintained. The
church land-receivers are not xnen of their word.
So give the Maoris back the land obtained under
false promises. The allegations have been estab-
lished to the satisfaction of a Parliamentary com-
mittee. Maori corollary: How can we believe
these men about the “ Kingdom of Heaven ” when
they lie about the kingdom of earth ?

Our Higher Civilisation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sheriff was younger than his brother by
five minutes; their united ages amounted to 4G
years. He happened to ask Norah first, else she
might have accepted his twin, but this time
Murray was five minutes late. When she told
linn that_ Sheriff was already successful, he ex-
pressed, first regret, secondly satisfaction, that she
was still to come into the family. Norah said she
didn’t mind which of them she married, as long as
she got one.
Sheriff had some curious theories regarding
marriage. He held that, with highly-civilised
natures, the chief tie was between mind and
mind ; all the rest, however pleasant, was
superfluous. He did not say it outright; but ho
led Norah to believe that he married her simply
because to have married any other woman would
have been to raise a ridiculous barrier between
himself and his “chum;”
Norah heard him with calmness, but seemingly
without conviction.
* • * •
A dull day, a crowded church ; bride,
father, clergyman waiting for the bridegroom. A
message had just been despatched to liis lodging;
A journalist by profession. Sheriff had been hard
at work up to the vefy day of his marriage ; for
convenience’ sake he had been spending the last
few weeks apart from his family;
When the messenger returned with a note Nordh
was in the vestry, where she had taken refuge with
her father and Murray. She accepted the note
With fingers that did not tremble. Incoherent,
badly written, it was still clearly from Sheriff,
and it told her that she had never been
really loved, that he had discovered his mistake iri
time, and when she received this he would be far
away. •
She gave it to het father, then to her brother-in-
law that was to be.
After the first angry shame Murray reflected.
“ I know how this will end,” he said, “you’ll
marry me—eventually; because I’m the best
suite Ito you—after him. Why not walk out and
do it now ? ”
Outside they were playing the voluntary for the
third time.
* * * *
It was the fourth day of the honeymoon.
(Somebody has said that the honeymoon lasts four
days.) The young couple were seated at breakfast
in their hotel parlor, when the door opened and
Sheriff Fraser entered the room.
She knew at once. “It was all a mistake,” she
cried.
Sheriff placed his carpet-bag on a chair. “ I
know,” he observed; “I was off my head;
scarcely slept for weeks bsfore, trying to get my
novel finished and everything eff my hands. I’ve
a vague remembrance of writing a lot of rot and
then clearing out. I’ve been up-country these last
few days.”
He was gkiicirig from oiie to the other as he
spoke. He had braced himself up for the shock
of seeing them together; he forgot that he had
often seen them together before, He had told
himself a dozen times that Norah was lost to him;
but—somehow—she did not seem to be lost to him ;
his heart gave a sudden bound as he reflected that
they could still read together. “Lost!” He re-
peated the word out loud.
Norah was before him, trembling.
“I’d give her back if I could,” interposed
Murray, somewhat nervously.
“And, after all, what have you really lost?”
added Norah, plucking up courage. “Nothing
that you wanted very much. I can still talk to
you and argue with you and sympathise with you.
You’re not a sensualist, Sheriff, or a fool. Dreams
of the mutual frying-pan and the joint-stock dust-
bin were never in your line. Am I lost to you
because you don’t get quite everything? ”
“ And, anyhow, we’ve got her into the family,”
added Murray, by way of consolation.
* * * *
Hot, palpitating with terror at the tragedy
he could hardly be in time to prevent, Norah’s
father stood outside the door of the hotel sitting-
room. How preternaturally quiet it was inside !
A rattle of a teaspoon, the sound of a chair
pushed back, and then the voice of Sheriff Fraser
as he turned to leave the room. “ Bring Norah
lack to Sydney soon, Murray ; I want her to look
over that poem of mine, ‘Love’s Vengeance.’ I
ton’t think I’ve put quite enough passion in it.”
“Cold-blooded young dog,” thought Norah’s
f ather, unreasonably disappointed as he glanced
lack at his own young days.
But when Nature and Civilisation grapple,
Civilisation does win—sometimes.
Maoriland.

"A Failure.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a poor, weak-backed fellow, spoilt when
a child by the dull respectability of his parents,
and shipped off to “ the colonies ” with an outfit
containing plenty of white shirts, but not enough
practical knowledge to enable him to keep them.
He drifted, like many other victims of ignorant
respectability, to the Auckland gum-fields.
“ A spade and a spear and a sack for a pikau are
all you want,” said a fellow traveller, and he got
these things by pawning his last trinket.
His respectability prevented him mixing with
rough people, consequently he missed his chance
of learning the practical rules of gum-digging.
He upheld his dignity in the solitude of a miser-
able whare which some misanthrope had built at
the bottom of a gully opening into the Manukau;
and blundered by accident on it patch of swamp
gum which kept him going until the winter rains
raised the water-level. The water gave him a
chill, for he had not the “Savee” to put fresh
thatch on to the roof, and the floor and his blan-
kets got damp.
His last purchase at the store was a tin of
molasses and two loaves of bread. He lived on
these fof a week, whilst he fought with influenza,
and then, driven by necessity; he went out on the
desolate hills muttering like a doddering old man :
1 ‘ I must get enough guin to buy food—l fnust, I
must.”
He said this ds if his “must” would influence
Providence to put gum in his way;
Pushing through wet manuka shdulder-highj he
climbed a clay slope, spearing as he went; driving
the sharp steel into the ground with desperate
fierceness; liiuttering, as his arms ached and his
head spun, “ I must, I must get gum ! ”
He stood on a barren ridge, straightened his
back, and saw the great Manukau Harbour
stretching sandbanks and swirling channels south-
ward into misty distances. A damp, cold wind
swept down from the Titirangi Ranges, and chilled
him to the bone ; but his head burned with feyet
and the desire in that frantic “ I must, I must.”
His heart gave a bound. The sharp steel point
went crunching into brittle resin. He set eagerly
to work with the spade and turned up a few lumps
Of gum. He dug on, but there was no more. He
scraped the gum roughly with his pocket-knife,
and then, hiding his spade and spear near the foot-
track, he set off on a four-mile walk to the store.
The store was principally hotel. Mr. Landlord
weighed the gum and threw down eighteenpence.
“ You look bad, Mister,” he said ; “ have a drink.”
The unfortunate ‘ 1 Mister ” chose brandy, and felt
its influence steal through every vein in his body,
“dive me another,” he said, with some of his old
affectation, “ and haveone with me;”
He drank his share at a gdlp, threw down a
shilling : “ Now give me a big loaf;”
He paid for the loaf with his only sixpenbe and
staggered out of the siore;
“If you are catching ahjr. schnapper bff the
r&cks I’ll give you sixpence apiece for them. And
iffy missis likes pipis, so I’ll pay for a kit if you
'will bring them up,” shouted the landlord after
him. Then it struck the poor unfortunate for the
first time that he had been living for weeks by a
harbor teeming with fish, oysters, and pipis.
The wind had risen and it was raining; but
whilst the brandy lasted he was oblivious to wet
or cold.
It grew dark, and he began to feel as if he had
walked many scores of miles. The rain was
trickling down his spine : his boots were full of
water and weighed tons, the track had gone
wrong somehow. Suddenly he stepped for a
moment into space, and came crash on to a clay
slide. He grabbed frantically at nothing and
went rolling and sliding down a bare slope, flop
into rushing water. It was only a gully flood, and
in the daylight a child could have scrambled out;
but this man’s struggles only resulted in send-
ing him downward more swiftly. Influenza
and brandy had addled his poor brain. He
was carried by the flood under arches of tangled
trees and bowers of giant ferns, wonderfully beau-
tiful in the sunlight, but fearsome caverns in the
darkness. Once he was jammed against the roots
of an old kauri, and could have climbed out within
cooee of a hut euchre-party ; but the water banked
against him, welled over him, spun round him,
and carried him onward. He had no hope of
escaping. One great idea was in his mind—he did
not know how to live ; but be knew how to die.
One short fight and he could be free of the water;
but the miniature flood tumbled him along, rolled
him out on the beach, and he lay there on the mud
until the tide rose, and the little crabs and the
curious fishes examined him and voted him a
failure.
F. ROLLIT.
In California, lately, a death - sentenced
assassin of sanguine hue feigned lunacy. The
Lunatic Asylum governor, disguised as a prisoner,
companioned with him, and he was executed on
this officer’s report.
Tetanus (lock-jaw and its kin) is now
known for certain to be due to a microbe, which,
like that of leprosy, lives in the soil. A curious
confirmation of this is the prevalence of tetanus
among farm-hands, grooms, gardeners, and parti-
cularly grave-decorators and cemetery men. Ceme-
tery hands, whenever they happen to wound
themselves in any way, plug the cut with grave-
yard earth, hence the large percentage of grave-
yard patients in proportion to their limited
numbers.
Said by a most expert analyst before the
British Liquor Commission The fusel oil in
whisky (which, by the way, rarely amounts to one
part in 1000) is not an impurity, but a necessary
ingredient naturally formed during fermentation
along with the pure • alcohol. It is the fusel oil
which gives the characteristic flavor, and, if it
were entirely removed, the result would be
ordinary ‘ silent spirit ’ (spirits of wine) quite des-
titute of flavour. The strong flavor of ‘ pot still ’
whisky is the effect of the fusel oil not being re-
moved, whereas in the milder-flavored ‘ patent
still ’ whisky much of the fusel oil has been separ-
ated off. The potencies of the two are not pro-
portionate to the fusel oil in them, but to the
amount of alcohol present. Also whisky does not
lose its fusel oil in ageing; old whisky contains
just as much as new.’

“ASPI NALL’S.”
South Sea Florists,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
miBS the #“ risls .” «« Maud,
“ 'to have annihilated her.
x Jon.sts, I echoed scornfully, “with floral
beauty littering the ground ! ”
Yes, that s just it —littering the ground,” she
pursued calmly; “ 1 prefer it gathered up.”
here ’ 1 suppose people can make bouquets
have seen one specimen: so can
ffthe Ei 1 "* 0 lhe S “ loon - “ ”“*> W

“So l perceive ” and Maud’s glance travelled

iii es ’ 111 s «®>” laughed Maud as the warning-

de fVYnon a ” IVeS ' wdl be rOWJC then ’ and cr ?™ e
Waud ! You don’t seem to realise that we
aiego-mg to live out of touch with civilisation,
among natives who adore Nature, who are inno-
cent of artifice, who never conceal their real feel-
mg's, who arc destitute of conventions ”•
4 Visitors ashore ! ”
My energy transferred itself from my tongue to
my toes, but as I settled down in the little boat
alongside the Irisco mail-steamer Maud’s merry
voice floated down from the deck :
* i there’s a woman just gone down with
f, om ® dy ® on her face, and here’s a man with pow-
dered hair. Hadn 11 better bring vou a few com-
plexion-bottles from Paris? They’ll be better
than native cosmetics.
Put if we had only been six days in Apia, Maud
had only stayed as many hours, and couldn’t pos-
sibly know what she was talking about.
1 smiled pitifully.
* * * *
Florists, indeed ! Oh, the delight of getting
away from their wires and gum, their unnatural
bouquets and emblems and wreaths ! The florist
Is a civilised abomination, only equalled by the
ci vilised garden. Now, here we were, idealising
Jite in an arbor, with ferns gracefully drooping
rioni the roof, and pretty girls, a blossom stuck
carelessly into their locks, were bringing us hand-
inJs of flowers in old salmon-tins, and everything
and everybody was in close sympathy with Nature
unadorned, and therefore at her best.
These were my satisfactory reflections when we
had successfully fled out of touch with civilisation
and were ensconced in the native hut which was to
be our home for months. Sugarcane thatch shel-
tering our heads, cocoa-nut mats for chairs and
lounges, breadfruit-leaves for plates, and novelty
lor spice—a perpetual picnic with none of the con-
ventional acoessoires. We had come pour nous
retremper, to hark back to ancestral, patriarchal
simplicity, to learn what we were before Progress
had put us into unnatural garments, mental and
physical. And we meant to go back, better and
wiser, but not sadder, for the experience.
I thought of Maud, sailing away on her ’Priseo
boat towards the pomps and vanities of the weary
old world. I smiled pitifully.
* * * *
The poetry of Nature lay about us like
Heaven in our infancy. Next day we came home
from a romantic walk, our arms laden with tropical
treasures and our brains with tropical fancies. We
were met by the pretty girls, dancing and spring-
ing towards us, garlands on their heads and a
nouth-organ in full career. The mouth-organ was
oinoying; Pan’s pipe seemed more appropriate.
But one cannot get rid of all trace of Progress, and
modern music was supposed to add a charm to
the siva which was offered to us—a children and
girls siva , the most innocent little performance
possible, with never a trace of the doubtful about
it; an open-air siva, with the setting sun-glow for
1 nnelight and green palms for background. Arca-
dia come back to us.
Would I be the siva queen ? Of course I would.
And straightway bent my head for the wreath,
fragrant thyme, and held out my hand for the
perfumed fan that was my sceptre, and down we
squatted and dreamily watched the pretty move-
ments of our gracious little brown maids. Inno-
cence disporting itself without vanity; sweetness
and light radiating from the untutored savage on
our world-worn spirits.
Again I thought of Maud, who would soon be
watching sivas of a very different nature ; Maud
with a society smile on her lips, and comfortless
raiment skewering her into fashion-plate sem-
blance, and wired blossoms in her hair, and in her
hand a fan plucked from the ill-used ostrich. I
still smiled pitifully.
* * * *
Maud’s fan recalled my own little sceptre,
and I took it up for inspection. Very fragrant
very diminutive very curious-so curious that I
toigot to watch the siva. Tiny fragments of
radiant color on tiny spikes—what could it be?
As I peereil curiously a conviction slowly arose
which made me go hot with vexation : those spikes
those bits of color—alas for disillusions The
palm-tree had been brought into play, the “flowers
of the forest ” rifled, but there Nature ended. My
simple little fan was of palm-frond ribs, on which
were spiked flower-petals, dainty, fragrant little
petals, ruthlessly mangled and skewered, one by
one. Never was civilised florist guilty of such
senseless barbarism.
Instinctively my hand went up to my head and
withdrew the wreath for examination: shredded
blossoms again. Gardenia petals, Cape jasmine
coiollas, broken tufts of native thyme, scraps of
leaf, all strung together, instead of the natural
. sprays I supposed myself to be wearing. And
the girls pretty wreaths were, no doubt, all equally
unnatural ; and Miss Simplicity over there, with
her virginal white coronal, its tiny pendants show-
ing like pearls on her broad forehead—she had
doubtless torn up sensitive petals with a view to
this innocent effect.
I believe I still smiled pitifully. The pity was
addressed to myself.
* * * *
But one gets over disillusions to start
afresh. After all, Progress could not be expected
to leave Arcadia untainted. There was surely
enough Nature and Poetry left to satisfy the most
captious. Two days later I saw them in combina-
tion, . adapting fait- fibre to native requirements.
I*itu is one of that admirable hibiscus family which
combines beauty with use : its yellow blossoms arc
pleasant to the eye, its yellow fibre is employed in
more ways than one. On this occasion it was
being converted into tit is, the flapping girdles that
grace ceremonial days.
.Iwas distressed. The tit is I had seen were of
vivid draciena leaves, besides which the straw-
colored fibre paled into insignificance. I said so.
Oh, but I didn’t understand. The fibre was to
be colored, making lovely tit is fa pea (like this).
And two gaudy things were held up for admira-
tion green and orange, blue, violet and red, a
girdle of bunched favors with fluttering streamers.
1 was interested at once : what native trees gave
the dye?
Ah, they no longer used native dyes, it was too
much trouble and the others were so much prettier
—English Judson’s !
This time I forgot to smile.
* * * *
After all, I had a chance of seeing the
native dyes at work. Passing a boatshed one day
we noticed the two fattest women in the village
pivoting to and fro on a slung bar. They were
apparently enjoying an impromptu merry go-
round m their dingy waistcloths and sloppy busts
a hibiscus stuck jauntily behind the ear, a native
cigarette protruding from the thick lips. The
cordage which slung their bar was dingy like
themselves, but of a chocolate dinginess, and the
drip-drip that fell into the oblong wooden bowl
was also ehoeolate-hued.
What were they doing ? What should they be
doing but screwing the colored juice out of the
leaves of the O’a tree, which were crushed inside
that cordage. Had 1 not noticed the warm-tinted
patterns on the best siapo, the siapo worn by chiefs
on great days? That was dyed with O’a. '
I his was charming. The dear native, with
happy-go-lucky ease, getting Nature to help him
in Ins simple manufactures and innocent class dis-
tinctions. How deeply interesting !
A plump young girl came up, mainly clad in
said plumpness. The leaf-girdle flapping round
her waist and the fishing-net on her shoulder
showed that she was out on business, but her
lustrous skin was well oiled, a necklace of
round capsicums like large cherries circled her
throat, and her short mahogany hair stood up stiff
and shiny. Very curious shade of hair, the kind
that suddenly became fashionable in Paris a few
years ago. I inspected it with such interest that
one of the pivotees removed her sului to give an
explanation.
“ O’a,” she said, laughing and pointing said
cigarette, first at the dye in the bowl, then at the
mahogany hair.
I became reflective. It would have been frivo-
lous to smile.
* * * «
My next experience was on a Saturday,
when most good native Christians—all natives here
are good Christians—prepare suitably for the Sab-
bath by plastering their hair white. The coral
lime is credited with bleaching properties, but it
is alsoeleansing and thereby necessarily connected
with Godliness. Those women who preserve then-
long curly locks don’t plaster, for obvious reasons,
but they lather with leaves from the Toa tree and
use the juice of lime and wild orange, and are con-
sequently as clean as their shorn neighbours,
borne of them, moreover, are independent of coral,
boasting a natural growth of light or even piebald
hue. I saw one of these natural growths on a
straight-profiled lass with unusually fair skin : it
stood out like a halo and was caught in at the top
of the head in bird-nest fashion, a perfect mop of
fuzzy tow.
“I suppose she values it highly,” I said, think-
ing of our society belles.
Yes, indeed. She was growing it specially for
sale. Each crop was worth a fine mat to her, the
most aristocratic skirt a brown damsel can own.
But this damsel was specially ambitious, and her
dream was a shark’s-tooth necklace.
Disconcerting, but innocent. Girls will be girls,
and after all sharks’ teeth were more beneficent
than “oysters’ tears.” If she liked to sacrifice
her chevelure for a parure, she was no worse than
your simple Breton and Polish peasant, only she
drove a better bargain.
“ And that one ? ” I asked, pointing to another
flaxen-haired, light- com plexioned nymph. Her
hair was curiously dark about the roots, and her
draperies had a dirty yellow tinge. “Does she
sell for mats ? ”
“Ah, no, she was less fortunate ; her hair was
not yellow: she stained it with turmeric, and it
was turmeric that gave her that soft yellow skin.
But it was nasty stuff to come off, and stained all
your lava-lavas.
Aureoline and Jaune de Ninon among our
primitive natives !
I had no looking-glass, but I believe myself to
have smiled sorrowfully.
* * * *
One grows hardened. I became used to it
all. Used to going' into raptures over a parti-
colored blossom which I discovered to bo the
corolla of a pink flower stuck into the corolla of a
white one. Used to admiring double blooms that
proved to be single blooms communistically ar-
ranged. Used to accepting necklaces of shred
petals and capsicum peelings and torn leaves, all
arranged to simulate that which they were not.
Pernaps my greatest trial was when a bunch of
soft yellow fans was brought to me and I disco-
vered never a stalk among them ; they were all
“ wired ” on coeoanut ribs.
I learned to accept it philosophically. I forgot
the art of smiling pitifully.
* * * *
When Maud passed on her way back I did
not go to meet the mail-boat—l bad not the face.
But I wrote to her :
“Come down here and set upas a florist and
perfumer. A few civilised lessons in the apprecia-
tion of Nature would improve barbarian art.”
0 . . Rose de Bohejie.
Siufanga, Navigator Islands.
Hutchison, M.H.R., on Premier Seddou,
of M L. :
The Premier has the capacity of an ostrich for pickin”'
up tulics, but nature lias denied him the power of assiniil
lation. lie, also, like the ostrich, when pursued by the
hunters, conceals his head in a mass of figures, though in
doing so he exposes his least honorable parts.
Sentencing Judge at Orange (N.S.-W.)
Quarter Sessions: “Anything known of this
man? His face seems familiar. ” Police: “Yes,
y’r Honor. (Soanso, soanso, &c.) And y’r Honor
sentenced him three years ago at Bathurst ’’—the
next “ sessions ” town ! “ to 15 years’ penal ser-
vitude.’ How do the “ infiuentials ” work the
oracle ?
Alfred Austin, Royal Rhymer, was special
correspondent for London Standard durino- the
I raiico-German war. It is hard to believe°that
any man who had ever witnessed the mount and
charge of heavy squadrons coqld have written
such a spiritless, short-footed, string-haltino-
waddle as that entitled “ Dr. Jim’s Ride.” But"
perhaps Alfred is a humorist with satirical spots. ’
Something interesting ought to happen
when the N.S.W. statistics for 1895 and 1896 are
available. Premier Reid, in his financial state-
ment, quoted figures showing the immense increase
of employment in certain industries under Free-
trade, but as these figures are not available to the
general public, and won’t be available for a long
time, no one could check bis assertions. M'Millan
alleges, however, that he inquired at the Statisti-
cian’s office and was informed “that the figures
given by the Premier were not the figures of
the statistician.” Consequently, M‘M. is wait-
ing in ambush for the publication of these statistics
and hopes to have a really good time with Reid
when they come out.
Ihe late Dr. Aubrey Bowen, of Melbourne,
writes a Bulletin correspondent,, predicted that
the great oculists of the future would be women,
whose fingers, naturally more slender and deft
than men’s, are better educated in “ finnicking ”
work. And, as for the alleged superiority of a
man’s nerve in surgical operations, it is an advan-
tage which he sacrifices to drinking and smokum
habits. The lady-doetor doesn’t clutch the knife
or the needle with an unsteady hand, whereas the
average whisky and cigar-loving sawbones is quite
incapable of threading a needle in a dim light.
Sometimes lie can’t guide a latchkey into his front
door,
The Long Arm of Coincidence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some years ago in a Maoriland city I attended an
inquest on a man who had carried about a bullet
in Ins brain for several days before succumbing to
toe injury. Ihe jury having found their verdict,
a toiv of us, including a doctor or two, the police-
inspector, the coroner, and the foreman of the
juiy, remained chatting in the hotel parlor, and
the conversation naturally turned on fatalities
similar to that which had been the subject matter
of the afternoon s business.
T J remarkable case I ever knew,” said
i)r. Ik, a retired military surgeon, “was that of
Oaptam Herford, an oificer in the colonial forces
who was wounded while gallantly leading on his
men at the siege of Orakau— the same engagement
that made the name of Rewi Maniapoto famous.
Her ford was struck by a bullet between the left
eye and the bridge of the nose. There was no
sign of the ball externally, and, anyhow, from the
position of the wound it was impossible to probe
tor it. However, as the poor fellow didn’t seem
much the worse for the wound, it was concluded
that he had been struck with a spent ball which
iiad caused a superficial fracture but bad not
penetrated. In a few days Herford was up and
about with a dressing on the wound, and, havin'-'
been promoted for bis bravery, lie spent several
weeks squaring* up his company’s accounts and
banding the command over to his successor. One
day, six weeks after the engagement, life was sud-
denly taken ill and went off pouf ! The autopsy
showed that the bullet had penetrated and travelled
round the inside of the cranium to the back of his
head, where it caused a fatal abscess.” (Which,
by the way, was exactly how JVTr. Norton the
Dubbo police-magistrate," died, after livirm in full
consciousness many months after he had been shot
by ms friend, the handsome Mrs. Wilkie.)
“I can tell you a more wonderful case than
commenced the foreman of the jury, but as
Oi. Ix. was also full of a reminiscence, the layman
courteously waved to him to proceed.
“Well, gentlemen,” said Dr. K., “I had the
privilege while in China of meeting the doetoi
who attended on W alters, one of Gordon’s men—
shot right through the body, just above the median
line. Dm ball entered just here,” said the doctor,
his hand on the last button of his waist-
*T a b and came out at the back, about an inch to
the left of the spinal column. This case, you may
not know, gentlemen, is recorded by our medical
authorities as one of the most remarkable in the
history of surgery. Walters recovered; though,
a,s it is more than 30 years ago, he may now be
dead.
“Doctor, ’ chipped in the foreman, who, by the
way, was a stranger to all present; “ I remember
hearing of that case. In tolling the story, I think
you made one or two little mistakes. If I re-
member right, the bullet came out at the right
hand side of the spine -you said it was the left—
and, moreover, the mail’s name was not Walters :
it was Walker.”
Ig K., who was a trifle pompous, didn’t seem
to like being corrected by a layman on a matter
of medical lore—he suggested that the foreman’s
memory was at fault.
“Doctor,’' said the foreman, “I’ll bet drinks
foi all bands that I am right, and that I can prove
it m a couple of minutes.”
Dr. K. smiled incredulously, but assented to the
wager with a rather patronising little nod.
I iist of all, said the foreman, diving his
hand into his breast pocket and producing some
faded papers “ here is the discharge of the very
man from the ‘ Ever Victorious Army,’and here
is a medical certificate signed by the surgeon you
Vi 1 on see, the name of the man was
Walker, and not ‘Waiters.’ ”
Then, to the surprise of the company, the fore-
man hurriedly removed his coat and vest, raised
his slurt, and, pointing to a cicatrice to the right
of his spine, remarked quietly: “ Pay for tin-
groceries, doctor. I’m ex-sergeant Walker, of
Gordon s force, and I’m the man that the bullet
went through.”
“ How is that for a coincidence ? ” said the in-
spector. _ “You wouldn’t believe it if you read it
in a novel.”
